The Seventh Argument: Supplication as Rudeness
Presents the seventh argument: supplication resembles correcting God, which is gross impropriety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument against supplication, which claims that supplication implies correcting God and is therefore a breach of etiquette.

Core Idea
Supplication can be seen as rudeness toward God if it implies He is neglectful.

This lesson presents a harsh critique of supplication: that it resembles waking up someone who is unaware or teaching someone who is ignorant. The argument claims that by asking, the supplicant implicitly accuses God of failing to be generous, which is considered extreme bad manners. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's direct and strong language.
2
Understand the comparison
The argument compares supplication to awakening the heedless and informing the ignorant. Ask yourself: what does this analogy imply about the supplicant's view of God?
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that this is 'an exaggeration in bad manners.' Consider why the author considers it so severe.
This connects to the broader theme of adab towards Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: إن الدعاء يجري مجرى تنبيه الغافل وإعلام الجاهل. فكأنه يقول لمعبوده: تركت الجود والفضل والرحمة فأنا أعلمك أن الأولى فعله. ولا شك أن هذا مبالغة في سوء الأدب.
The seventh argument: Supplication runs in the course of awakening the heedless and informing the ignorant. As if he says to his worshipped: You left off generosity, bounty, and mercy, so I inform you that the best course is to do it. There is no doubt that this is an exaggeration in bad manners.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection, which critiques supplication from the perspective of etiquette towards God. It compares supplication to waking up someone who is unaware or teaching someone who is ignorant. The supplicant, by asking, implicitly suggests that God has failed to be generous, bountiful, or merciful, and that the supplicant knows better what should be done. This is considered a serious breach of proper conduct towards the Divine. Thus, supplication is seen as disrespectful.

Key Terms
الدعاء
تنبيه الغافل
إعلام الجاهل
سوء الأدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Supplication implies that God is neglectful and needs to be reminded.
Correction: In Islamic theology, supplication is an act of worship that recognizes our need for God, not a correction of His actions.

Checkpoint

A) Thanking and praising.
B) Awakening the heedless and informing the ignorant.
C) Commanding and prohibiting.
D) Confessing sins and repenting.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument against supplication claims that it resembles awakening the heedless and informing the ignorant, thus being an act of bad manners towards God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If supplication is purely worship, then every word you utter in du'a should be an act of humility. But are your du'as filled with requests more than praise and submission? How can you reshape your du'a to reflect true dependence on Allah?

The Argument from Royal Etiquette
Presents the eighth argument: etiquette before a king forbids direct asking, so it is even more fitting before God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth argument against loud supplication, which draws an analogy from courtly etiquette before a powerful king.

Core Idea
If you don't ask a king directly, how can you ask God directly?

The eighth objection appeals to common sense about respect before authority. When in the presence of a powerful ruler, direct requests are considered impolite. This argument claims that the same etiquette applies, even more so, in the presence of God. Let's examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: a single sentence states the rule, then applies it to God.
2
Understand the analogy
Identify the two parts: the rule before a sultan, and its application before God. Ask yourself why the author thinks this analogy is valid.
The key term 'ذي الجلال' (the Majestic One) emphasizes God's supreme authority.
3
Recognize the implied conclusion
The argument concludes that aloud supplication with direct asking is disrespectful. Why would this be an objection against loud supplication?
The objection targets the act of verbalizing requests, not internal prayer.
4
Consider the potential counter
Think about how this objection might be answered. Does God command supplication? If so, does that override the etiquette analogy?
This prepares you for the author's response in the next section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة: إن من حضر في مجلس السلطان القاهر فإن الأدب أن لا يشافهه بالطلب. ورعاية هذا الأدب في حضرة ذي الجلال أولى.
The eighth argument: Whoever attends the court of a powerful sultan, proper etiquette is that he should not address him directly with a request. Observing this etiquette in the presence of the Majestic One is more fitting.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and final objection. It draws an analogy from worldly etiquette: when attending the court of a powerful sultan, it is considered impolite to make direct verbal requests. If this is true for a human ruler, then it is even more appropriate to observe such reverence in the presence of God, who is far more majestic. Therefore, aloud supplication that involves direct asking is seen as improper and thus ineffective or even disrespectful.

Key Terms
السلطان القاهر
الأدب
الطلب
ذي الجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is always disrespectful to make direct requests to God.
Correction: While respect is due, God commands believers to supplicate to Him directly, as in the Qur'an 'Call upon Me, I will respond to you' (40:60). This argument may be refuted by the fact that God encourages direct supplication.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth argument, what is the proper etiquette before a powerful sultan?
A) To make requests loudly.
B) To make requests through an intermediary.
C) Not to address him directly with a request.
D) To present gifts instead of requests.

Key Takeaway
The eighth argument uses etiquette before a king to argue that direct verbal requests to God are improper, though this may be countered by God's command to supplicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you ever hold back from asking God directly because you feel too insignificant? How does this argument expose the hidden pride in thinking you know how to approach God better than He commanded?
?
If you were standing before a king, you would weigh every word. When you pray, do you weigh your words carefully from reverence, or do you rush through them? What would change if you truly believed you were standing before 'the Majestic One'?
?
The argument suggests silence is more respectful. Yet God commands you to call upon Him. Which attitude governs your heart: the fear of overstepping or the trust in His invitation? Are you willing to examine which one is really about humility and which is about distance?

The First Benefit of Du'a: Turning the Soul to God
Introduces the first benefit: how du'a transforms the soul through divine immersion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how du'a transforms the soul by redirecting it from worldly pleasures to divine contemplation, resulting in spiritual happiness.

Core Idea
Du'a benefits the soul by immersing it in God's majesty, not by changing His decree.

The author now presents the first benefit of du'a from the perspective of those who affirm its spiritual efficacy. This passage begins with a psychological observation about the soul's innate attraction to worldly pleasures and then shows how du'a reorients the soul toward God. The transformation described is the core benefit of supplication.

Guided Path
1
Understand the soul's natural inclination
Notice how the author describes the soul as inherently disposed to love bodily pleasures and immediate goals. This sets up the problem that du'a addresses.
The soul's nature (firra) is key here.
2
Recognize the challenge of turning to God
The author states that turning entirely to God is contrary to the soul's habit and nature. This highlights the difficulty and the need for a motivating factor.
3
See how du'a initiates the turn
The supplicant's belief that there is a purpose in du'a causes the soul to turn toward God's majesty and persist in request.
The 'purpose' could be any need or spiritual goal.
4
Observe the resulting immersion
Persistence in du'a leads to the soul becoming immersed in acknowledging God's perfect power and will throughout the cosmos.
5
Connect to the outcome: happiness and detachment
The text concludes that this process brings happiness, and greater perseverance leads to greater detachment from the lower world.
Detachment (inqita') is a key spiritual concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعترفون بأن الاشتغال بالدعاء : طريق نافع ، ومنهج مستقيم فقد ذكروا فيه فوائد : الفائدة الأولى : إن النفس بمقتضى جوهرها [ وموجب فطرتها (٣) ] : مجبولة على حب اللذات الجسمانية ، والأغراض العاجلة . وأما الإعراض عن هذه الطريقة ، والإقبال على الله بالكلية ، فعلى خلاف عادتها ، وعلى مناقضة فطرتها . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الداعي إذا اعتقد أن له في الدعاء غرضاً . فلاجل تحصيل ذلك الغرض تتوجه النفس إلى جانب جلال الله تعالى ، وتصر وتلح [ في ذلك الطلب (١) ] وذلك الإصرار والإلحاح يوجب استغراق النفس في الإقرار بكمال قدرة الله تعالى ، ونفاذ () قدرته ومشيئته في جميع أجزاء العالم الأعلى والأسفل . وبهذا الطريق يحصل للنفس هذه السعادة ، وكلما كانت المواظبة على هذه الطريقة أكثر، كان الانقطاع عن العالم الأسفل أكثر
As for those who acknowledge that engagement in supplication is a beneficial way and a straight path, they have mentioned benefits in it: The first benefit: The soul, by virtue of its essence and the requirement of its nature, is disposed to love bodily pleasures and immediate objectives. And turning away from this way and turning entirely to God is contrary to its habit and opposes its nature. If you know this, we say: The supplicant, if he believes that he has a purpose in supplication, then for the sake of obtaining that purpose, the soul turns to the side of the majesty of God, and persists and insists in that request. And that persistence and insistence necessitate the soul's immersion in acknowledging the perfection of God's power and the penetration of His power and will in all parts of the higher and lower world. And by this way the soul obtains this happiness, and the more one perseveres in this method, the greater the detachment from the lower world.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage begins the response of those who affirm the benefit of du'a. It introduces the first benefit. The argument starts with a psychological observation: the soul is naturally inclined toward bodily pleasures and immediate worldly aims. Turning away from these and turning fully to God is contrary to its habitual nature. Given this, when a supplicant believes he has a purpose in du'a, his soul turns toward the majesty of God and persists in request. This persistence and insistence cause the soul to become immersed in acknowledging God's complete power and the penetration of His will throughout the entire cosmos, both higher and lower. Through this process, the soul attains true happiness, and the more one persists in this method, the greater the detachment from the lower world. Thus, du'a is beneficial not because it changes God's decree, but because it transforms the soul.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The benefit of du'a is primarily in changing external circumstances (e.g., getting what one asks for).
Correction: This passage shows that the primary benefit is internal: turning the soul toward God, immersing it in His majesty, and producing spiritual happiness and detachment from the world.

Checkpoint

A) Frustration and disappointment.
B) Immersion in acknowledging God's power and will.
C) Attachment to worldly pleasures.
D) A sense of entitlement.

Key Takeaway
Du'a benefits the supplicant by redirecting the soul from worldly pleasures toward immersion in God's majesty, resulting in spiritual happiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is your du'a primarily a means to get what you want, or a means to immerse yourself in God's majesty? What would change if you treated du'a as an end in itself?
?

Introduction and Two Premises on Visiting the Dead
Author introduces a king's question and presents two foundational premises about souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational premises that the author establishes for the discussion on benefiting from visiting the dead, and the rationale behind the second premise regarding the strength of separated souls.

Core Idea
Separated souls gain direct knowledge of the unseen, making visiting the dead beneficial.

In this opening of the eighteenth chapter, the author recounts a king’s question about visiting the dead and sets down two premises that underpin the entire discussion. The first premise affirms the survival of human souls after bodily death, while the second compares the cognitive powers of separated souls with those still attached to bodies. These premises lay the groundwork for understanding how the living can benefit from the dead.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter opening
Begin by reading the passage where the author describes the king who asked the question. Notice the respectful tone and the author's intention to summarize his treatise.
The king is Muhammad ibn Sam al-Ghuri.
2
Identify the two premises
Look for the phrases 'المقدمة الأولى' and 'المقدمة الثانية'. Understand what each premise claims about human souls.
The first premise states souls persist after bodily separation.
3
Analyze the comparison of souls
The second premise compares separated and attached souls. Note the two aspects: separated souls are stronger in some ways (revealed knowledge), attached souls stronger in others (not fully explained here).
The author explains that upon separation, the veil is lifted, making demonstrative knowledge necessary.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why these premises are necessary for discussing visiting the dead. How does the survival and enhanced knowledge of souls make interaction possible?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن عشر
في
بيان كيفية الانتفاع بزيارة الموتى
قال المصنف (۱) : سألني بعض أكابر الملوك عن هذه المسألة
الملك : محمد بن سام بن الحسين الغوري ، وكان رجلا حسن السيرة ، مرضي الطريقة ، شديد الميل إلى العلماء ، قوي الرغبة في مجالسة أهل الدين والعقل فكتبت له فيه رسالة ، وأنا أذكر ههنا ملخص ذلك الكلام . فأقول : الكلام فيه مبني على مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : إنا قد دللنا على أن النفوس البشرية ، باقية بعد مفارقة الأبدان
والمقدمة الثانية (۲) : إن تلك النفوس التي فارقت أبدانها أقوى من هذه النفوس المتعلقة بالأبدان من بعض الوجوه . وهذه النفوس أقوى من تلك من وجه آخر . أما أن النفوس المفارقة أقوى من هذه النفوس من بعض الوجوه : فهو أن تلك النفوس لما فارقت أبدانها ، فقد زال الغطاء والوطـاء . وانكشف لها عالم الغيب ، وأسرار منازل الآخرة. فصارت العلوم التي كانت برهانية عند التعلق بالأبدان : ضرورية بعد مفارقة الأبدان . وكانت تلك
The Eighteenth Chapter: On Explaining How to Benefit from Visiting the Dead. The author said: One of the great kings asked me about this issue. The king: Muhammad ibn Sam ibn al-Husayn al-Ghuri, and he was a man of good conduct, praiseworthy way, strongly inclined toward scholars, and had a strong desire for sitting with people of religion and intellect. So I wrote a treatise for him on it, and I mention here a summary of that discourse. So I say: The discussion on it is based on premises: The First Premise: We have demonstrated that human souls remain after separation from bodies. And the Second Premise: Those souls that have separated from their bodies are stronger than these souls attached to bodies in some respects. And these souls are stronger than those in another respect. As for the separated souls being stronger than these souls in some respects: it is that those souls, when they separated from their bodies, the covering and the veil were removed. And the unseen world and the secrets of the abodes of the Hereafter were revealed to them. So the knowledges that were demonstrative when attached to bodies became necessary after separation from bodies. And those were...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a new chapter: 'On Explaining How to Benefit from Visiting the Dead.' The author begins by stating that a great king, described as virtuous and inclined toward scholars, asked him about this issue. In response, the author wrote a treatise and now provides a summary. He then states that the discussion is built on premises. He gives the first premise: human souls survive after leaving the body. This is a foundational claim for the possibility of benefiting from the dead. The second premise begins: separated souls are stronger in some ways than souls still attached to bodies, while attached souls are stronger in other ways. The author explains the former: upon separation, the covering is removed, revealing the unseen world and secrets of the afterlife. Knowledge that was only demonstrative becomes necessary (self-evident) for the separated soul. The passage ends here, continuing onto the next page. The argument is structured as a preamble to a larger discussion about how the living can interact with the dead.

Correction: The author argues that separated souls have enhanced knowledge of the unseen, making visiting them beneficial.

C) Because they no longer need bodies to think.
D) Because they are closer to God.

Key Takeaway
Separated souls, free from bodily covering, gain direct necessary knowledge of the unseen, which forms the basis for the possibility of benefiting from visiting the dead.

Three Premises on the Survival and Nature of Souls
Razi enumerates three premises on soul survival and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three foundational premises about the survival of human souls, the relative strengths of separated and embodied souls, and the soul's passionate attachment to the body.

Core Idea
Souls survive death, but their strengths shift—the dead gain direct knowledge, the living keep learning.

Before explaining the benefit of visiting graves, Razi establishes three essential premises about the soul. These premises define the soul's survival, its altered capabilities after death, and its persistent love for its body. Understanding these will prepare you for the applied argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the premises as a logical foundation
Approach the passage with the mindset that Razi is building a case. Notice that each premise begins with 'المقدمة' and serves as a building block.
The first premise is stated very briefly, as the proof is assumed known.
2
Compare separated vs. embodied souls
In the second premise, Razi makes a comparative argument. Note the two opposite strengths: separated souls gain direct knowledge, embodied souls retain the means to acquire new knowledge.
This contrast is crucial for later application.
3
Understand the passionate attachment
The third premise describes the soul's love-like attachment to its body, which remains after death. Ask yourself: Why does this matter for visiting graves?
This idea directly supports the application in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام فيه مبني على مقدمات:
المقدمة الأولى: إنا قد دللنا على أن النفوس البشرية باقية بعد مفارقة الأبدان.
والمقدمة الثانية: إن تلك النفوس التي فارقت أبدانها أقوى من هذه النفوس المتعلقة بالأبدان من بعض الوجوه، وهذه النفوس أقوى من تلك من وجه آخر. أما أن النفوس المفارقة أقوى من هذه النفوس من بعض الوجوه: فهو أن تلك النفوس لما فارقت أبدانها فقد زال الغطاء والوطاء، وانكشف لها عالم الغيب وأسرار منازل الآخرة. فصارت العلوم التي كانت برهانية عند التعلق بالأبدان ضرورية بعد مفارقة الأبدان. وكانت تلك النفوس الروحانية حين كانت النفوس بدنية كانت تحت غبار وبخار، فلما زال البدن أشرقت تلك النقوش وتجلت وتلالات، فحصل للنفوس المفارقة عن الأبدان بهذا الطريق نوع من الكمال.
وأما أن النفوس المتعلقة بهذه الأبدان أقوى من تلك النفوس المفارقة من وجه آخر: فلأن آلات الكسب والطلب باقية لهذه النفوس. فهذه النفوس بواسطة الأفكار المتلاحقة والأنظار المتعاقبة تستفيد في كل يوم علماً جديداً وبحثاً زائداً. وهذه الحالة غير حاصلة للنفوس المفارقة.
والمقدمة الثالثة: إن تعلق النفوس بأبدانها تعلق يشبه العشق الشديد والحب التام. ولهذا السبب فإن كل شيء يطلب تحصيله في الدنيا فإنما يطلب ليتوصل به إلى إيصال الخير والراحة إلى هذا البدن. وإذا ثبت هذا فإذا مات الإنسان وفارقت النفس هذا البدن فذلك الميل يبقى وذلك العشق لا يزول إلا بعد حين. وتبقى تلك النفس عظيمة الميل إلى ذلك البدن عظيمة الانجذاب إليه، لا سيما على المذهب الذي نصرناه من أن النفوس الناطقة مدركة للجزئيات وأنها تبقى موصوفة بهذا الإدراك بعد موت البدن.
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمات فنقول: إن الإنسان إذا ذهب إلى قبر إنسان قوي النفس كامل الجوهر شديد التأثير ووقف هناك ساعة وتأثرت نفسه من تلك التربة حصل لنفس هذا الزائر تعلق بتلك التربة. وقد عرفت أن لنفس ذلك الميت تعلق بتلك التربة أيضاً. فحينئذ يحصل لنفس هذا الزائر الحي ولنفس ذلك الإنسان الميت ملاقاة بسبب اجتماعهما على تلك التربة. فصارت هاتان النفسان شبیهتين بمرآتين.
The discussion therein is built on premises:
The first premise: We have indeed demonstrated that human souls remain after separation from bodies.
The second premise: Those souls that have separated from their bodies are stronger than these souls attached to bodies in some respects, and these souls are stronger than those in another respect. As for the separated souls being stronger than these souls in some respects: it is that when those souls separated from their bodies, the veil and covering were removed, and the world of the unseen and the secrets of the abodes of the afterlife were disclosed to them. So the sciences that were demonstrative while attached to bodies became necessary after separation from bodies. And those spiritual souls, when they were bodily souls, were under dust and vapor; when the body was removed, those engravings shone, became manifest, and glittered, so for the souls separated from bodies, through this way, a kind of perfection is obtained.
As for the souls attached to these bodies being stronger than those separated souls in another respect: it is because the instruments of acquisition and seeking remain for these souls. These souls, through successive thoughts and consecutive reflections, acquire new knowledge and additional research every day. And this state is not present for the separated souls.
The third premise: The attachment of souls to their bodies is an attachment resembling intense passion and complete love. For this reason, everything that is sought in this world is sought only to reach the means of bringing good and comfort to this body. And if this is established, then when a person dies and the soul separates from this body, that inclination remains and that passion does not disappear except after some time. And that soul remains intensely inclined to that body, intensely attracted to it, especially according to the position we have advocated that rational souls perceive particulars and remain described with this perception after the death of the body.
And if you have understood these premises, we say: When a person goes to the grave of a person with a strong soul, perfect substance, intensely influential, and stands there for a while, and his soul is affected by that soil, the soul of this visitor obtains an attachment to that soil. And you have known that the soul of that deceased also has an attachment to that soil. Then there occurs for the soul of this living visitor and the soul of that deceased a meeting due to their joining on that soil. So these two souls become like two mirrors.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents a three-part argument explaining the metaphysical benefit of visiting the graves of the dead. It begins by stating the discussion is built on premises. The first premise asserts the survival of human souls after death, which is taken as already demonstrated. The second premise compares the strengths of separated souls (those of the dead) and embodied souls (the living). Separated souls gain direct knowledge of the unseen, making their knowledge necessary rather than demonstrative, and they achieve perfection. However, embodied souls have the advantage of instruments of acquisition (reason, thought) to gain new knowledge daily, which separated souls lack. The third premise describes the intense love-like attachment of the soul to its body. This attachment persists after death for a period, so the deceased soul remains strongly inclined to its body/grave. After presenting these premises, the author applies them: when a living person visits a grave, his soul becomes attached to the soil of that grave, which already has an attachment from the deceased's soul. Thus the two souls meet on that soil, becoming like two mirrors—reflecting and interacting. The benefit of visiting the dead is this spiritual connection.

Key Terms
النفوس البشرية (human souls)
النفوس المفارقة (separated souls)
العشق الشديد (intense passion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe the soul ceases to exist or becomes completely inert after death, disconnected from the body.
Correction: Razi argues that the soul survives, gains some perfections, and retains an emotional attachment to its body.

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, in what way are separated souls stronger than embodied souls?
A) They can acquire new knowledge every day.
B) They have direct knowledge of the unseen after death.
C) They still have instruments of earning and seeking.
D) They are free from all attachment.

Key Takeaway
Razi's three premises establish that souls survive, have different strengths in life and death, and retain a passionate attachment to their bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says that after death, souls lose the ability to acquire new knowledge through reflection. If your time to learn in this life is limited, what are you actively doing today to seek knowledge that will benefit you in the afterlife? Are you putting off learning about your faith until it's too late?
?
The soul's attachment to the body is described as an intense passion that persists after death. Think about your own attachments to your body—your appearance, health, and comfort. If that attachment remains after death, how does that affect your view of preparing your body for the grave? Are you more concerned with worldly adornment or with ensuring a good state when that attachment turns to longing?
?
Razi notes that separated souls gain direct knowledge of the unseen but cannot perform new acts of worship or learning. When you stand before your Lord with the knowledge you have now, will it be enough? Does this lesson stir in you a sense of urgency to act on what you know, because the time for action will soon end?

The Spiritual Benefit of Visiting Graves: The Mirror Analogy
Razi applies the three premises to argue for spiritual benefit of visiting graves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that visiting the grave of a spiritually powerful person facilitates a meeting of souls through their common attachment to the soil, like two mirrors facing each other.

Core Idea
Visiting graves creates a meeting of souls via their shared attachment to the soil.

Having established that souls survive, have specific strengths, and remain attached to their bodies, Razi now applies these premises to explain the benefit of visiting graves. The key is that both the visitor's soul and the deceased's soul are attached to the same soil, allowing a spiritual interaction like two mirrors reflecting each other.

Guided Path
1
Connect the premises to the application
Recall that the deceased soul is strongly attached to its body/soil. Now consider the visitor: his soul also becomes attached to that soil when he visits. This joint attachment is the basis for interaction.
Razi uses the word 'تعلق' (attachment) as key concept.
2
Understand the mirror analogy
The two souls become like two mirrors: they face each other through the soil, allowing reflection and influence. Think about how mirrors reflect light—here the souls reflect each other's presence.
This is a metaphysical concept, not physical.
3
Reflect on prerequisites for benefit
The visitor must go to a person 'قوي النفس كامل الجوهر شديد التأثير' (strong soul, perfect substance, intensely influential). Not all graves are equal; the deceased must have spiritual power.
This explains why some visits are more beneficial.

Source Text

?

?

?
If the meeting of souls through grave soil is real and mutual like mirrors, what should change in your behavior when you visit graves? Will you now go with the intention of a real connection, not just recitation? Make a specific commitment.

The Philosophical Explanation for the Benefit of Visiting Graves
The author gives three premises about souls and uses them to justify the benefit of grave visitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical rationale behind the legitimacy of grave visitation, based on the nature of souls and their mutual spiritual exchange.

Core Idea
Grave visitation is a two-way spiritual exchange between the living and the dead.

This passage presents a sophisticated philosophical argument for why visiting the graves of the righteous is spiritually beneficial. The author grounds this practice in the nature of souls—both separated and attached—and their persistent attachment to the body even after death. By understanding these premises, the mutual benefit of visitation becomes clear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Read the passage carefully and locate the three distinct premises about souls: (1) disembodied souls achieve a kind of perfection, (2) embodied souls are stronger in acquiring knowledge, and (3) the soul's attachment to its body persists after death like intense love.
These premises set the foundation for the argument.
2
Understand the connection through the grave
Notice how the author uses the soil of the grave as a meeting point. Both the visitor's soul and the deceased's soul have a connection to that soil, enabling a spiritual meeting.
The soil becomes a medium of exchange.
3
Grasp the two-mirror analogy
Visualize two polished mirrors facing each other, reflecting light. Similarly, the visitor's acquired knowledge and virtues reflect to the deceased, and the deceased's honorable knowledge reflects back to the visitor.
This mutual reflection is the core benefit of visitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفوس الروحانية حين كانت النفوس بدنية كانت تحت غبار وبخار، فلما زال البدن أشرقت تلك النقوش، وتجلت وتلالات، فحصل للنفوس المفارقة عن الأبدان، بهذا الطريق: نوع من الكمال. وأما أن النفوس المتعلقة بهذه الأبدان، أقوى من تلك النفوس المفارقة من وجه آخر: فلات آلات الكسب والطلب باقية لهذه النفوس. فهذه النفوس بواسطة الأفكار المتلاحقة، والأنظار المتعاقبة، تستفيد في كل يوم علماً جديداً، وبحثاً زائداً. وهذه الحالة غير حاصلة للنفوس المفارقة. والمقدمة الثالثة: إن تعلق النفوس بأبدانها، تعلق يشبه العشق الشديد والحب التام. ولهذا السبب فإن كل شيء يطلب تحصيله في الدنيا. فإنما يطلب ليتوصل به إلى إيصال الخير والراحة إلى هذا البدن. وإذا ثبت هذا، فإذا مات الإنسان، وفارقت النفس هذا البدن. فذلك الميل يبقى، وذلك العشق لا يزول إلا بعد حين. وتبقى تلك النفس عظيمة الميل إلى ذلك البدن، عظيمة الانجذاب إليه. لا سيما على المذهب الذي نصرناه من أن النفوس الناطقة مدركة للجزئيات، وأنها تبقى موصوفة بهذا الإدراك بعد موت البدن. وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمات، فنقول: إن الإنسان إذا ذهب إلى قبر إنسان، قوي النفس، كامل الجوهر شديد التأثير، ووقف هناك ساعة، وتأثرت نفسه من تلك التربة. حصل لنفس هذا الزائر تعلق بتلك التربة. وقد عرفت: أن لنفس ذلك الميت، تعلق بتلك التربة. أيضاً. فحينئذ يحصل لنفس هذا الزائر، الحي، ولنفس ذلك الإنسان الميت: ملاقاة، بسبب اجتماعهما على تلك التربة. فصارت هاتان النفسان شبیهتين بمرآتين، صفيلتين، وضعنا بحيث ينعكس الشعاع من كل واحدة منهما إلى الأخرى. فكل ما حصل في نفس هذا الزائر الحي من المعارف البرهانية، والعلوم الكسبية، والأخلاق الفاضلة من الخضوع الله تعالى، والرضا بقضاء الله تعالى، ينعكس منه نور إلى روح ذلك الإنسان الميت، وكل ما حصل في نفس ذلك الإنسان من العلوم المشرفة والآثار القوية الكاملة فإنه ينعكس منها نور إلى هذا الزائر الحي. وبهذا الطريق تصير تلك الزيارة سبباً لحصول المنفعة روح الكبرى، والبهجة العظمى لروح الزائر، ولروح المزور. فهذا هو السبب الأصلي في شرعية الزيارة. ولا يبعد أن يحصل فيها أسرار أخرى أدق وأغمض مما ذكرناه. وتمام العلم بحقائق الأشياء ليس إلا عند الله سبحانه وتعالى.
The spiritual souls, when the souls were bodily, were under dust and vapor. Then when the body ceased, those engravings brightened, and became clear and radiant, so for the souls separated from bodies, through this path, a type of perfection is obtained. As for the souls attached to these bodies, they are stronger than those separated souls from another aspect: because the instruments of acquisition and seeking remain for these souls. These souls, through successive thoughts and alternating reflections, gain every day a new knowledge and additional research. And this state is not obtained for the separated souls. The third premise: The attachment of souls to their bodies is an attachment resembling intense passion and complete love. For this reason, everything that one seeks to obtain in the world is only sought to thereby bring good and comfort to this body. And when this is established, then when a person dies and the soul leaves this body, that inclination remains, and that passion does not disappear except after some time. And that soul remains intensely inclined toward that body, intensely attracted to it. Especially according to the doctrine we have supported that rational souls perceive particulars, and that they remain described with this perception after the death of the body. And when you have understood these premises, we say: That if a person goes to the grave of a person strong in soul, perfect in substance, intense in effect, and stands there for an hour, and his soul is affected by that soil, then for the soul of this visitor a connection with that soil is obtained. And you have known that for the soul of that dead person also there is a connection with that soil. Then for the soul of this living visitor and for the soul of that dead person a meeting occurs due to their both being on that soil. So these two souls become like two mirrors, two polished ones, placed such that the ray reflects from each one to the other. So whatever is obtained in the soul of this living visitor of demonstrative knowledge, acquired sciences, and virtuous morals of submission to Allah Almighty and contentment with Allah's decree, light reflects from it to the spirit of that dead person, and whatever is obtained in the soul of that person of honorable sciences and strong perfect effects, light reflects from them to this living visitor. And through this path, that visitation becomes a cause for obtaining the great benefit and immense joy for the spirit of the visitor and the spirit of the visited. This is the original reason for the legitimacy of visitation. And it is unlikely that there occur in it other secrets more subtle and obscure than what we have mentioned. And complete knowledge of the realities of things is only with Allah Subhanahu wa Ta'ala.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof for the spiritual benefit of visiting the graves of the righteous. It begins with two preliminary observations: first, that disembodied souls achieve a kind of perfection once freed from the body's 'dust and vapor'; second, that souls still attached to bodies are actually stronger in an important sense because they can continually acquire new knowledge through thought and reflection. The third premise is that the soul's attachment to its body is like intense love, which persists after death. Using these premises, the author argues that when a living person visits the grave of a righteous person, both the visitor's soul and the deceased's soul are connected through the soil. This leads to a mutual 'meeting' and exchange: the visitor's acquired knowledge and virtues reflect like light to the deceased, and the deceased's honorable knowledge and strong effects reflect back to the visitor. Thus, visitation becomes a cause for mutual benefit and joy. The author concludes that this is the original reason for the legitimacy of visitation, while acknowledging that there may be deeper secrets known only to Allah.

Key Terms
النفوس الناطقة (rational souls)
الزيارة (visitation)
تعلق (attachment)
المعارف البرهانية (demonstrative knowledge)
الأخلاق الفاضلة (virtuous morals)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that visiting graves only benefits the visitor or is merely a ritual without real spiritual exchange.
Correction: The passage teaches that both the living visitor and the deceased receive mutual spiritual benefits through the reflection of knowledge and virtues.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the original reason for the legitimacy of visiting graves (ziyara)?
A) To request favors from the dead
B) The mutual reflection of spiritual benefits between the visitor and the deceased
C) To fulfill a religious obligation
D) To receive blessings directly from the grave

Key Takeaway
The legitimacy of grave visitation is grounded in the mutual spiritual exchange between the visitor and the deceased, facilitated by their shared connection to the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author describes a mutual benefit. Have you ever considered that your ziyara might be a source of joy for the deceased? How does this responsibility affect the way you prepare your soul before visiting a grave?

The Mutual Spiritual Benefit in Ziyarah
Author describes mutual spiritual reflection in ziyarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mechanism of mutual spiritual exchange between the visitor and the deceased during ziyarah as explained by the author, and why this forms the basis for its legitimacy.

Core Idea
Ziyarah is a two-way spiritual reflection: both visitor and deceased benefit through light and knowledge.

This lesson presents the author's unique explanation of how ziyarah benefits both the living visitor and the deceased. Rather than a one-way prayer, the author describes a mutual reflection of light and knowledge between two souls. The passage uses an analogy of two reflecting layers to illustrate this spiritual exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the analogy of two reflecting layers at the beginning.
2
Understand the analogy
The author sets up an image of two layers reflecting light. This is the foundation for the exchange. Ask yourself: what do the layers represent?
3
Identify the two flows
Identify the two directions of spiritual light: from the visitor to the deceased, and from the deceased to the visitor. Note the specific elements (knowledge, morals, etc.) that are reflected.
The passage lists demonstrative knowledge, acquired sciences, and virtuous morals for the visitor; noble knowledge and perfect effects for the deceased.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states that this mutual benefit is the primary reason for the legitimacy of ziyarah. Consider how this explanation differs from a purely one-way view.
The author also hints at deeper secrets, showing humility before God's complete knowledge.

Two layers, we placed them so that the ray reflects from each one to the other. So whatever occurs in the soul of this living visitor of demonstrative knowledge, acquired sciences, and virtuous morals—such as humility before God Almighty and contentment with God's decree—light reflects from it to the soul of that dead person. And whatever occurs in the soul of that person of noble knowledge and strong, perfect effects, light reflects from them to this living visitor. And by this way, that visitation becomes a cause for obtaining the benefit of the great spirit and the supreme joy for the visitor's spirit and for the visited spirit. So this is the original reason for the legitimacy of visitation. And it is not far that there occur in it other secrets more precise and hidden than what we mentioned. And complete knowledge of the realities of things is only with God, the Glorious and Exalted. (1) from (ص). (2) and more worthy (س).

Commentary
This passage presents the author's explanation of the spiritual mechanism underlying the legitimate visitation of graves (ziyarah). It opens with a simile of two reflecting layers, establishing the principle of mutual reflection of light between the visitor and the deceased. The author then describes two flows: first, from the living visitor's soul—bearing demonstrative knowledge, acquired sciences, and virtuous morals like humility before God and contentment with divine decree—light reflects to the deceased's soul. Second, from the deceased's soul—containing noble knowledge and perfect effects—light reflects back to the visitor. This reciprocal exchange renders the visitation a cause for great spiritual benefit and supreme joy for both souls. The author concludes that this is the primary reason for the legitimacy of visitation (shar'iyyat al-ziyarah), adding that there may be even more subtle secrets beyond what has been mentioned, while affirming that complete knowledge of realities belongs solely to God.

Correction: The passage shows it is mutual: both visitor and deceased reflect spiritual light to each other, resulting in benefit for both.

The Soul as King and the Body as Kingdom
The author compares the soul to a king, the body to a kingdom, and introduces two armies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical analogy of the soul as a king and the body as his kingdom, and how the two armies (visible organs and inner powers) assist in perfecting the soul and body.

Core Idea
The soul's journey from ignorance to knowledge depends on the senses as its first army.

The philosophers often used vivid analogies to explain the relationship between soul and body. In this passage, the author presents the first example: the soul is like a king, the body like his kingdom, and he has two armies. This lesson unpacks that analogy and begins to show how the soul uses sensory perception to acquire knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the analogy and its parts sink in.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-explanation.
2
Identify the two armies
The passage describes two armies: one visible to the eye (external and internal organs) and one seen by insight (the powers embedded in those organs). Note the distinction.
This is key to understanding how the soul interacts with the body.
3
Understand the first type of assistance
The first type of assistance relates to the soul's perfection through knowledge. The soul is created without knowledge, so it uses the senses to perceive objects and discern similarities and differences.
The explanation cuts off here, but the main idea is presented.

Know that the philosophers have mentioned many examples in this chapter. The first example is that the substance of the soul is like a king, and the body is like his kingdom. This king has two armies: an army seen by sight, which is the external and internal organs, and an army seen by insight, which is the powers embedded in those organs. And know that these powers assist sometimes in perfecting the soul's interests and other times in perfecting the body's interests. Commentary
This passage introduces the first example from a series by the philosophers. The author compares the substance of the soul (جوهر النفس) to a king, and the body to his kingdom. The king has two armies: one visible to the eye—the external and internal organs—and another visible to insight (البصيرة)—the powers (القوى) embedded in those organs. The author then states that these powers assist in two ways: sometimes in perfecting the soul's interests, and other times in perfecting the body's interests. The passage begins to explain the first type of assistance: the rational soul's perfection is in knowing truth for itself and good for action. However, action on good requires prior knowledge, and the soul is created without knowledge. Therefore, it is given the external and internal senses so that through sensory perception it can discern similarities and differences among objects. The text cuts off before completing this explanation.

Scriptural Proofs for Animal Intelligence
Cites four Quranic verses as scriptural evidence that animals possess understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how four Quranic verses are used as scriptural evidence for the claim that animals possess intelligence.

Core Idea
The Quran itself shows that animals communicate and reason.

This lesson presents the scriptural (naqli) evidence for animal intelligence. The author cites four verses from Surah An-Naml, where animals speak and act rationally. These verses are taken as direct proof that animals have understanding. Let's examine each verse and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
2
Identify the four verses

3
Understand the logical reasoning
For each verse, consider why the author uses it as evidence. Pay special attention to the comment on the third verse: 'this threat is not fitting except for rational beings.'
4
Consider the student's interpretation
The author mentions a student's interpretation that 'speech of birds' might refer to the invocation of Mercury. Think about how this alternative interpretation relates to the argument.
This is a side note, not central to the main proof.
5
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
Consider how these four verses together build a case for animal intelligence from scripture.

As for scriptural proof: they have adhered, in proving their view, to verses: The first: His saying, the Exalted, recounting from Solomon: 'O people, we have been taught the speech of birds, and we have been given of everything. Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural proof (النقل) for the view that animals possess intelligence. It cites four Quranic verses: (1) Solomon's statement that he was taught the speech of birds (Surah An-Naml 27:16). The author notes a student's interpretation that 'speech of birds' might refer to the invocation of Mercury (a planet). (2) The ant's warning to the others (27:18). (3) Solomon's threat to punish the hoopoe for absence (27:20-21), which the author argues is only appropriate with rational beings. (4) The hoopoe's report from Sheba (27:22), which implies understanding and communication. These verses are used to argue that animals have intellect and communication, supporting the rational conclusion from the previous examples.

Scriptural and Transmitted Evidence for Animal Intelligence
The author cites Quranic verses and a transmitted anecdote to support the claim that animals are intelligent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Quranic verses, a hadith, and an anecdote to support the claim that animals possess intelligence.

Core Idea
Animals are intelligent – the Quran and tradition prove it.

The author has already argued rationally for animal intelligence; now he turns to scriptural and transmitted evidence. This passage compiles six Quranic verses, a narration from Imam al-Baqir, and a remarkable anecdote about a gazelle, all pointing to the same conclusion. As you read, notice how each piece of evidence highlights a different aspect of animal awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. The passage is longer than usual, so take your time.
Pay attention to the structure: verses first, then a hadith, then an anecdote, then a conclusion.
2
Identify the six Quranic verses
The author lists six distinct verses. For each, ask: what does it show about animal intelligence? For example, the threat to the hoopoe implies rationality.
3
Examine the transmitted reports
The hadith about sparrows and the anecdote about the gazelle are not from the Quran but from tradition. Reflect on why the author includes them.
The gazelle story is a personal anecdote cited from a book.
4
Understand the concluding argument
The supporters of animal intelligence argue that since animals perform subtle actions, it is no stretch to say they know their Lord. Consider how this connects the evidence to the main claim.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقل: فقد تمسكوا في إثبات قولهم بآيات: الأولى: قوله تعالى حكاية عن سليمان: ﴿يا أيها الناس علمنا منطق الطير وأوتينا من كل شيء إن هذا لهو الفضل المبين﴾. وسمعت بعض تلامذتي يقول: لا يبعد أن يكون المراد من تعلم منطق الطير هو دعوة عطارد. الثانية: قوله تعالى: ﴿حتى إذا أتوا على وادي النمل قالت نملة يا أيها النمل ادخلوا مساكنكم﴾. الثالثة: قوله تعالى: ﴿وتفقد الطير فقال مالي لا أرى الهدهد أم كان من الغائبين؟ لأعذبنه﴾ وهذا التهديد لا يليق إلا مع العقلاء. الرابعة: قوله تعالى حكاية عن الهدهد: ﴿أحطت بما لم تحط به وجئتك من سبأ بنبأ يقين﴾. الخامسة: قوله تعالى: ﴿يا جبال أوبي معه والطير﴾. السادسة: قوله تعالى: ﴿والطير صافات كل قد علم صلاته وتسبيحه﴾ قيل: معناه كل من الطير قد علم صلاته وتسبيحه. قال بعضهم: كنت جالساً عند أبي جعفر الباقر فقال لي: أتدري ما تقول هذه العصافير عند طلوع الشمس وبعد طلوعها؟ قلت: لا أدري. قال: فإنها تقدس ربها وتسأله قوت يومها. وأقول: رأيت في بعض الكتب: أن في بعض الأوقات اشتد القحط وعظم حر الصيف والناس خرجوا للاستسقاء فلما فلجوا قال: خرجت إلى بعض الجبال فرأيت ظبية جاءت إلى موضع كان في الماضي من الزمان مملوءاً من الماء ولعل تلك الظبية كانت تشرب منه فلما وصلت الظبية إليه ما وجدت فيه شيئاً من الماء وكان أثر العطش الشديد ظاهراً على تلك الظبية فوقفت وحركت رأسها إلى جانب السماء مراراً فأطبق الغيم وجاء الغيث الكثير. ثم إن أنصار هذا القول قالوا: لما بينا بالدلائل أن هذه الحيوانات تهتدي إلى الحيل اللطيفة فأي استبعاد في أن يقال إنها تعرف أن لها رباً مديراً وخالقاً؟ فهذا تمام ول في دلائل هذه الطائفة.
As for transmitted evidence: they have clung in proving their statement to verses: First: His statement, the Exalted, relating from Solomon: 'O people, we have been taught the language of birds and have been given of all things. Indeed, this is the clear bounty.' And I heard some of my students say: it is not unlikely that what is meant by learning the language of birds is the call to Mercury. He said: Indeed, they glorify their Lord and ask Him for their daily sustenance. And I say: I saw in some books that at some times the drought intensified and the summer heat became severe, and the people went out to pray for rain. When they arrived? He said: I went out to some mountains and saw a gazelle coming to a place that in the past was full of water, perhaps that gazelle used to drink from it. When the gazelle reached it, it did not find any water in it, and the effect of severe thirst was apparent on that gazelle. So it stood and moved its head towards the sky repeatedly, then the clouds covered and much rain came. Then the supporters of this statement said: Since we have shown by proofs that these animals are guided to subtle tricks, what improbability is there in saying that they know that they have a Lord, a Director, and a Creator? This is the end of the discussion on the proofs of this group.

Commentary
This passage presents the transmitted (naqli) evidence for animal intelligence, consisting of six Quranic verses, a hadith from Imam al-Baqir, a personal anecdote, and a concluding argument. The six verses are: (1) Solomon being taught the language of birds; (2) the ant warning others; (3) Solomon threatening the hoopoe; (4) the hoopoe bringing news from Sheba; (5) addressing the mountains and birds with David; (6) stating that every bird knows its prayer and glorification. The author notes that the threat to the hoopoe is only appropriate if the hoopoe is rational. A student's interpretation that 'language of birds' might mean the call to Mercury is mentioned. Then a narration from al-Baqir interprets sparrows' chirping as glorification and supplication for sustenance. The author adds a story of a gazelle that prayed for rain by moving its head, resulting in rain. Finally, the proponents of animal intelligence conclude that since animals perform such subtle actions, it is not far-fetched that they know their Lord. This passage compiles multiple scriptural and traditional proofs to reinforce the rational argument.

Correction: The evidence shows animals possess intelligence and awareness, but the author does not claim it is identical to human intellect. It is a level of awareness suited to their nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Author states universal agreement on an evil spirit.

The author shows that this shared belief is so fundamental that it even shapes common liturgical phrases like seeking refuge from Satan. Let's explore how this universal agreement is presented.

Hippocrates on Spiritual vs. Physical Epilepsy
Author cites Hippocrates to show early philosophers acknowledged spiritual epilepsy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hippocrates's distinction between spiritual and physical epilepsy provides evidence that early philosophers acknowledged spiritual causes, countering later denial.

Core Idea
Early philosophers recognized spiritual epilepsy, later ones denied it.

This lesson examines a passage where the author cites Hippocrates to show that early philosophers did not universally deny evil spirits. Hippocrates, an influential physician-philosopher, distinguished between epilepsy treated by temple practitioners and that treated by physicians, implying both spiritual and physical causes. This challenges the assumption that all philosophers rejected spiritual influences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage opens with a statement about later philosophers agreeing to deny evil spirits, while the author is unsure of the earlier philosophers' position. This sets up a contrast.
Note that 'its denial' refers to the existence of evil spirits or spiritual epilepsy.
2
Read Hippocrates's citation
The author quotes Hippocrates's book on airs, waters, and places, where he says stagnant waters cause various diseases including epilepsy. Then Hippocrates adds a crucial clarification.
Hippocrates is an early philosopher-physician, so his view represents early philosophical thought.
3
Analyze the distinction
Hippocrates distinguishes between epilepsy treated by temple practitioners (spiritual) and that treated by physicians (physical). The author takes this as proof that early philosophers acknowledged a spiritual dimension.
This distinction directly contradicts the later philosophers' denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فالمتأخرون منهم اتفقوا على إنكاره . وأما المتقدمون : فلا أعرف مذهبهم في هذا الباب . إلا أني رأيت في کتاب ( الأهوية والبلدان ) لـ ( بقراط ) أنه ذكر فيه : أن المياه الواقفة تورث من العلل والأمراض كذا وكذا . ومن جملتها : الصرع . ثم قال : « لا أريد به الصرع الذي يعالجه أصحاب الهياكل ، بل الصرع الذي يعالجه الأطباء ، وهذا تصريح بأن الصرع قد يكون روحانياً وقد يكون جسمانياً
As for the philosophers, the later ones among them agreed upon its denial. As for the earlier ones, I do not know their doctrine in this matter, except that I saw in the book 'al-Ahwiya wa al-Buldan' by Hippocrates that he mentioned therein: Stagnant waters cause such and such illnesses and diseases, and among them is epilepsy. Then he said: 'I do not mean by it the epilepsy that the temple practitioners treat, but the epilepsy that physicians treat.' And this is a clear statement that epilepsy may be spiritual and may be physical.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage addresses the philosophers' stance. Later philosophers deny the existence of evil spirits, but earlier ones are less clear. The author cites Hippocrates, an early philosopher-physician, who in his book mentions that stagnant waters cause various diseases including epilepsy. But Hippocrates distinguishes between epilepsy treated by temple practitioners (suggesting a spiritual cause) and epilepsy treated by physicians (physical cause). The author takes this as evidence that early philosophers recognized a spiritual dimension. This shows that the denial of evil spirits is not universal among philosophers.

Correction: Later philosophers denied it, but earlier ones like Hippocrates acknowledged spiritual epilepsy alongside physical epilepsy.

First View: Jinn as Airy Bodies

Jinn are airy bodies — their penetration of bodies is as natural as air reaching our organs.

This passage introduces a key theological view about the physical nature of jinn. The author presents an analogy from everyday experience — inhaled air — to argue that jinn, being airy in essence, can enter and move within animal bodies. This view grounds the existence and activity of jinn in a concrete physical model.

This analogical reasoning makes the claim plausible.
5

The Second View: Jinn as Abstract Souls – Three Possibilities
Sanusi enumerates the second view on jinn and its three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view that jinn are abstract souls and the three possibilities for how they may possess attributes and interact with the physical world.

Core Idea
Jinn as abstract souls: three possibilities from pure intellect to subtle bodies.

The author continues his survey of views on the nature of jinn by presenting the second major position: that jinn are abstract souls (nufus mujarrada), not bodies nor residing in bodies. Under this view, there are three distinct possibilities for how these souls might function. This passage enumerates them, each offering a different account of their relationship to physical instruments and their similarity or difference to human souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the numbered list: 'the first', 'the second', 'the third'. Each presents a different possibility for abstract souls.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the three possibilities are complete.
2
Compare the first two possibilities on physical instruments
The first says they do not need physical instruments for attributes (knowing, powerful, etc.). The second says they do need them, but those instruments are subtle (air or ether) and their dissolution does not cause death, unlike human souls.
Note the analogy with human souls using subtle bodies in heart/brain.
3
Reflect on the third possibility and its implication
The third possibility mentions rational souls (nufus natiqa) that have left their bodies and become strong through sacred knowledge. This suggests jinn could be such souls. Think about how this connects to the overall discussion.
The text ends with 'qawiyat' (became strong), implying they are powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن يقال : إنها نفوس مجردة ، ليست أجساماً ، ولا حـالـة في الأجسام . وعلى هذا التقدير فههنا احتمالات . الأول : أن يقال : إنها عالمة قادرة سامعة مبصرة ، من غير أن يكون لها حاجة في حصول هذه الصفات إلى آلات جسمانية. وتلك النفوس بنوعياتها (٣) مخالفة للنفوس البشرية . والثاني : أن يقال : إنها تحتاج في كونها عالمة ، فاعلة إلى آلات جسمانية. إلا أن تلك الآلات جزء مخصوص من الهواء أو جزء (1) معين من كرة الأثير. وتبدد أجزاء تلك الأجسام وتحللها [ لا) ] يوجب موت تلك الأرواح . كما أن النفوس الإنسانية إنما تسمع وتبصر وتتفكر وتتخيل بواسطة أجسام لطيفة روحانية ، تتولد في القلب أو في الدماغ . ثم إن تلك الأجزاء تبدأ في التحلل والتفرق . ولم يلزم منه الطعن في كون الإنسان باقياً ، فكذا ههنا . الثالث : أن يقال : إن النفوس الناطقة التي فارقت أبدانها (٦) وبقيت دهراً داهراً ، وزماناً طويلاً في إشراق المعارف القدسية . لا شك أنها قويت
And the second: It is said that they are abstract souls, not bodies, nor residing in bodies. On this assumption, there are possibilities. The first: It is said that they are knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, without needing physical instruments for the acquisition of these attributes. And these souls by their types are different from human souls. The second: It is said that they need physical instruments to be knowing and acting, except that those instruments are a specific portion of air or a specific part of the sphere of the ether. And the disintegration of the parts of those bodies and their dissolution does not necessitate the death of those spirits. Just as human souls hear, see, think, and imagine by means of subtle spiritual bodies generated in the heart or brain. Then those parts begin to dissolve and separate, but that does not entail impugning the continued existence of a person. So likewise here. The third: It is said that the rational souls that have left their bodies and remained for a long time in the illumination of sacred knowledge – undoubtedly they have become strong...
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents the second view on jinn and devils: they are abstract souls, not bodies. Under this view, there are three possibilities. The first possibility: these souls possess knowledge, power, hearing, and sight without needing physical organs, and they are different from human souls. The second possibility: they do need physical instruments, but these instruments are made of a specific portion of air or ether. The dissolution of these instruments does not kill the spirit, just as human souls use subtle spiritual bodies in the heart/brain, and the dissolution of those parts does not destroy the human person. The third possibility: rational souls that have left their bodies and been illuminated by sacred knowledge for a long time become strong. The passage is cut off, but it implies that such souls could be what we call jinn. Each possibility offers a different explanation for how jinn can exist and act.

Key Terms
نفوس مجردة
آلات جسمانية
أثير
نفوس ناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abstract souls cannot interact with the physical world at all.
Correction: The second possibility shows that abstract souls may use subtle physical instruments like air or ether, allowing them to act without being embodied in the usual sense.

Checkpoint
According to the second possibility under the abstract souls view, what kind of instruments do jinn need?
A) Physical instruments made of fire
B) A specific portion of air or a part of the ether
C) Human-like bodies
D) No instruments at all

Key Takeaway
The second view on jinn posits them as abstract souls with three possibilities: entirely independent of physical instruments, dependent on subtle instruments, or as powerful rational souls from sacred illumination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The first possibility describes souls that know, hear, and see without any physical instruments. You rely on your body for these experiences. What would it mean to you to transcend physical limitations in the afterlife? Are you investing in the non-material faculties of your soul today?
?
The analogy in the second possibility says that the dissolution of subtle bodies does not destroy the soul. What are you attached to in this life that you think defines you? If those things dissolved, would your core self remain? What is the one thing you could lose and still be you?

Jinn and Shayateen as Branches of Celestial Spirits
Defines the third inquiry: jinn and shayateen as branches of celestial spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how jinn and shayateen are classified as branches of celestial spirits and the alternative religious view of the Supreme Spirit.

Core Idea
Jinn and shayateen stem from celestial forces or a Supreme Spirit, not independent.

This lesson presents the third inquiry of the chapter, which investigates the origin of jinn and shayateen. The author ties them to celestial spirits, explaining that evil spirits arise from Saturn and Mars, while good spirits come from Jupiter and Venus. He then contrasts this with the view of religious communities who see them as products of the Supreme Spirit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage to find the opening statement that jinn and shayateen must be products of celestial spirits. This is the core thesis of the inquiry.
2
Distinguish the two types
Notice the classification: harmful evil souls originate from Saturn and Mars, while good pure spirits come from Jupiter and Venus. Understand the astrological basis.
3
Contrast with religious view
Identify how religious communities differ: they posit a Supreme Spirit that exists independently and produces jinn and shayateen without celestial involvement.
This presents a theological alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبحث الثالث من المباحث المتعلقة بهذا الباب : أن هذه الأرواح المسماة بالجن والشياطين ، يجب أن تكون من نتاج الأرواح الفلكية ، ومن آثارها وشعبها. فالأرواح المتولدة المتشعبة من جوهر نفس « زحل » و « المريخ » هي المسماة بالنفوس الشريرة المؤذية . وأما الأرواح الخيرة السعيدة الطاهرة النقية ، فهي الأرواح المتولدة من أرواح السعديين وعلى هذا الباب فقس . وأما أرباب الملل والنحل فقانون قولهم يقتضي أن الروح الأعظم ، موجود برأسه ، ولا تعلق له بالأرواح الفلكية . والجن والشياطين شعبه ونتاجه وأولاده وأنصاره
And the third inquiry related to this chapter: that these spirits called jinn and shayateen must be from the products of the celestial spirits, and from their effects and branches. So the spirits generated and branching from the substance of the soul of Saturn and Mars are those called the harmful evil souls. As for the good, fortunate, pure, and clean spirits, they are the spirits generated from the spirits of the two fortunate [planets] (al-sa'diyayn). And upon this chapter, analogize. As for the people of religious communities and sects (arbab al-milal wa al-nihal), the rule of their statement requires that the Supreme Spirit (al-ruh al-a'zam) exists by itself, and has no connection to the celestial spirits. And the jinn and shayateen are its branches, products, children, and supporters.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines the third inquiry (al-bahth al-thalith) concerning jinn and shayateen. It begins by stating that these spirits must be the product, effects, and branches of the celestial spirits (al-arwah al-falakiyya). Specifically, spirits generated from the essence of the souls of Saturn and Mars are called harmful evil souls, while good, fortunate, pure spirits come from the spirits of the two fortunate planets (Jupiter and Venus). The author then notes the view of the people of religious communities and sects (arbab al-milal wa al-nihal), which holds that the Supreme Spirit (al-ruh al-a'zam) exists independently with no connection to celestial spirits, and that jinn and shayateen are its branches, products, children, and supporters. The passage maintains a distinction between a philosophical/astrological classification and the religious view.

Key Terms

الجن
الشياطين

النفوس الخيرة
الروح الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jinn and shayateen are entirely independent beings with no connection to celestial or spiritual forces.
Correction: This passage shows that they are either generated from celestial spirits (specifically from planetary souls) or from the Supreme Spirit, depending on the perspective.

Checkpoint

The First Proof Against Jinn: Dense or Subtle Bodies?
Presents the first argument: if jinn exist, they must be one of two body types, both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first argument against the existence of jinn and why both possibilities are considered false.

Core Idea
Jinn cannot exist because they would have to be either dense or subtle bodies, and both are impossible.

This lesson examines the first rational argument presented against the existence of jinn and shayateen. The argument sets up a simple dichotomy: if jinn exist, they must be either dense or subtle bodies. By showing that neither category can account for the properties attributed to jinn, the author concludes that jinn do not exist. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the logical connectors: 'if...then', 'both are false'.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Notice that the author presents two and only two possibilities for the physical nature of jinn: dense or subtle. This is a complete division.
In logic, this is called a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Examine the refutation of dense bodies
The author argues that if jinn were dense, they would be visible. If we accept invisible dense objects, we could doubt all sensory experience, leading to sophistry.
Sophistry (safsata) here means radical skepticism.
4
Examine the refutation of subtle bodies
For subtle bodies, two problems arise: they would be fragile (scattered by wind) and incapable of strenuous deeds attributed to jinn.
The argument appeals to physical plausibility.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since both possible body types are impossible for jinn, the existence of jinn is logically false. This completes the first proof.
The argument does not prove jinn do not exist absolutely, but within this rational framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إنها لو كانت موجودة ، لكانت إما أن تكون من الأجسام الكثيفة ؛ أو من الأجسام اللطيفة . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بوجودها . أما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها من الأجسام الكثيفة ، فلأنها لو كانت كذلك ، لوجب في كل من كان سليم الحس ، أن يبصرها. إذ لو جوزنا حضور مثل هذه الموجودات ، مع أنا لا نراها لم يمتنع أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة ، وشموس مشرقة ، ورعود قاصفة ، ورياح عاصفة أنا لا نحس بشيء منها . وذلك يوجب القول بالسفسطة . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها من الأجسام اللطيفة . فذلك لأنها لو كانت كذلك ، لتفرقت وتمزقت عند هبوب الرياح القوية العاصفة ، فكان يجب أن تموت بأسرع الأسباب ، وأقل الموجبات. وأيضاً : فالأجسام اللطيفة لا تكون قادرة على الأفعال الشاقة. والناس يصفونها بالقدرة على الأعمال الشاقة . ولما ثبت فساد القسمين ، ظهر أن القول بإثبات الجن والشياطين : باطل .
The first proof: If they [jinn and shayateen] existed, they would be either from dense bodies or from subtle bodies. Both divisions are false, so the statement of their existence is false. As for the explanation that it is impossible for them to be from dense bodies: because if they were like that, it would be necessary for everyone with sound sense to see them. For if we allowed the presence of such existents while we do not see them, it would not be impossible for there to be in our presence towering mountains, shining suns, crashing thunders, and violent winds of which we perceive nothing. And that leads to the statement of sophistry (safsata). As for the explanation that it is impossible for them to be from subtle bodies: that is because if they were like that, they would scatter and tear at the blowing of strong violent winds, so they would necessarily die by the quickest causes and the least occasions. Also: subtle bodies are not capable of strenuous actions, yet people describe them as capable of strenuous works. And when the corruption of both divisions was established, it became clear that the statement affirming the existence of jinn and shayateen is false.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-hujja al-ula) against the existence of jinn and shayateen. It proceeds by dichotomy: if jinn exist, they must be either dense bodies (ajsam kathifa) or subtle bodies (ajsam latifa). Both are shown to be impossible. For dense bodies: if they were dense, anyone with sound senses should see them; otherwise we would have to accept that we might not perceive huge mountains, suns, etc., which leads to sophistry (safsata). For subtle bodies: they would scatter in strong winds and die easily, and they would be incapable of the strenuous deeds attributed to them. Since both possibilities are false, the existence of jinn is false.

Key Terms
الأجسام الكثيفة
الأجسام اللطيفة
السفسطة
الحجة الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is that subtle bodies cannot exist at all.
Correction: The argument is not against subtle bodies per se, but against the specific combination of invisibility, durability, and power claimed for jinn.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given for why jinn cannot be subtle bodies?
A) They would be seen by everyone
B) They would be scattered by wind and die quickly
C) They would be too heavy to move
D) They would be luminous like stars

Key Takeaway
The first argument uses a dichotomy to show that jinn cannot exist because neither dense nor subtle bodies can account for the properties attributed to them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This rational argument concludes that jinn cannot exist because of logical contradictions. Yet, as a believer in the Qur'an, you affirm the existence of jinn. Does this tension trouble you? How do you reconcile your faith with the force of this proof?
?
If a non-Muslim presented this argument to you, would you be able to respond? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on revelation versus reason when it comes to the unseen?
?
The author claims that believing in invisible dense bodies leads to sophistry—denying the reliability of your senses. How often do you trust your senses in matters of faith? Are there beliefs you hold that, if examined rationally, might undermine your trust in God's signs?

Second Argument: Cursing Shayateen and Lack of Harm
Presents the second proof: if shayateen existed, they would harm their cursers, but they don't.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second empirical argument against the existence of shayateen based on the lack of observed harm from cursing them.

Core Idea
If shayateen existed, cursing them would bring harm; since no harm occurs, they don't exist.

This lesson presents a second proof that challenges the reality of shayateen using observable evidence. The argument reasons that if shayateen were real and powerful, they would harm those who curse them, especially the pious. Since no such harm is observed, the report about them must be baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: observation, conditional, conclusion.
2
Identify the key observation
The argument begins with an observed fact: Muslims unanimously curse shayateen and speak ill of them. This is the premise.
3
Analyze the conditional reasoning
The argument then uses a conditional: if shayateen existed, they would be harmed by the cursing. Moreover, if they could harm others, the virtuous would suffer more than the wicked.
This step shows the logical consequence that would follow if shayateen were real.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since no such harm is observed, the argument concludes that there is no basis for the report about shayateen. Consider why this conclusion follows from the premises.
The conclusion is empirical: absence of evidence is evidence of absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنا نرى المسلمين مطبقين على لعن الشياطين ، وإساءة القول فيهم ، ولو كانوا موجودين لتأذوا منهم . فإذا كانوا قادرين على الإضرار ، وجب أن يكون وقوع أهل الخير والدين في الضرر من جانبهم ، أكثر وأعظم من وقوع أهل الفساد فيه ، وحيث لم يظهر من هذا الباب أثر ولا خير ، علمنا : أنه لا أصل لهذا الحديث.
The second proof: We see the Muslims unanimously cursing shayateen and speaking ill of them. If they existed, they would be harmed by them. And if they were capable of causing harm, then it would be necessary that the occurrence of harm from their side upon the people of goodness and religion would be more and greater than its occurrence upon the people of corruption. And since no trace or good has appeared from this matter, we know that there is no basis for this report.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-hujja al-thaniya) against the existence of shayateen. It observes that Muslims unanimously curse shayateen and speak ill of them. If shayateen existed, they would be harmed by this cursing. Moreover, if shayateen had the power to harm, they would harm the virtuous more than the wicked. Since no such harm is observed, the report about shayateen has no basis.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is purely theological and not based on observation.
Correction: The argument is empirical: it relies on the observable fact that Muslims curse shayateen and that no harm results from it.

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses observable lack of harm to conclude that shayateen do not exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Will you repent from attributing your struggles to shayateen and instead turn to Allah alone with your fears and frustrations?
?

Ask yourself: am I willing to abandon a belief if reason and evidence show it has no foundation? What would that mean for how you live your faith?
