The Argument: Jinn Make Miracles Ambiguous
Presents the third argument: jinn make miracles attributable to them, thus removing the proof of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how belief in jinn undermines the proof of prophethood by making miracles attributable to unseen beings.

Core Idea
If miracles could be from jinn, prophethood becomes unprovable.

This lesson presents the third argument against jinn: if jinn exist, their unknown powers could account for any miracle, removing the distinctive proof that a prophet is sent by Allah. The author built on the consensus of religious communities and the necessity of distinguishing prophetic miracles from other phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from consensus to the problem of unknown jinn.
2
Identify the premises
The argument starts with the consensus that prophets are sent by Allah. Then it states that we do not know the jinn, their knowledge, or powers.
3
Follow the implication
Because jinn are unknown, any miracle could be from them, so no miracle can prove a prophet's claim.
4
Understand the conclusion
Thus, belief in jinn forces us to impugn prophethood (al-ta'n fi al-nubuwwa). The conclusion is that affirming jinn and devils necessitates rejecting prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن أرباب الملل والنحل أطبقوا على تصديق الأنبياء عليهم السلام في دعواهم : أنهم مبعوثون إلى الخلق من قبل الله تعالى . والقول بالجن يوجب فساد دعواهم . لأنا لا نعرف الجن. وإذا كنا لا نعرفهم ، لم تعرف مقادير علومهم ، ولا مقادير قواهم وقدرهم. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فلا نوع من أنواع المعجزات ، إلا ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك النوع ، من هؤلاء الجن والشياطين. وحينئذ لا يبقى دليل على صحة قولهم في ادعاء الرسالة . فثبت : أن الإقرار بالجن والشياطين ، يوجب الطعن في النبوة . فإن قالوا : هذا كلام ضعيف في دفع ذلك الإشكال القوي ، إذ لا يمتنع ممن يريد الإغواء والإضلال : أن يتحمل ذلك القدر من الطعن واللعن في تحصیل مراده .
The third proof: The people of religious communities and sects have unanimously agreed to affirm the prophets, peace be upon them, in their claim that they are sent to creation by Allah Almighty. And the statement about jinn necessitates the corruption of their claim. Because we do not know the jinn. And if we do not know them, we do not know the extents of their knowledge, nor the extents of their powers and capacity. On this estimation, there is no type of miracle except that it is possible that that type be from these jinn and shayateen. Then no proof remains for the validity of their statement in claiming prophethood. So it is established that affirming jinn and shayateen necessitates impugning prophethood. And if they say: This speech is weak in repelling that strong difficulty, for nothing prevents one who desires misguidance and misdirection from bearing that amount of defamation and cursing to achieve his goal.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-hujja al-thalitha) against the existence of jinn and shayateen. It begins by noting that all religious communities affirm the prophets' claims to be sent by Allah. Belief in jinn, however, corrupts that claim because we do not know the jinn – their knowledge, powers, and capacities are unknown. Consequently, any miracle could plausibly be attributed to jinn, leaving no proof for prophethood. Thus, affirming jinn necessitates impugning prophethood (al-ta'n fi al-nubuwwa). The passage then includes an objection from an opponent (فإن قالوا) dismissing this argument as weak, arguing that one who seeks misguidance would willingly bear defamation and cursing to achieve his goal. The author does not provide an explicit response in this text.

Key Terms
أرباب الملل والنحل
المعجزات
الطعن في النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument proves that jinn do not exist.
Correction: The argument does not prove jinn's non-existence; it only shows that belief in jinn is inconsistent with maintaining the proof of prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why does belief in jinn undermine the proof of prophethood?
A) Because jinn are more powerful than angels
B) Because miracles could be attributed to jinn rather than God
C) Because the prophets themselves believed in jinn
D) Because jinn are invisible

Key Takeaway
Belief in jinn, if taken seriously, makes it impossible to verify any prophet's claim because miracles could be attributed to jinn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever entertained doubts about the possibility of jinn influencing events, have you considered how that doubt might weaken your certainty in the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him)? In what specific area of your life have you allowed ambiguity to replace conviction?
?
The passage argues that ignorance of jinn's powers makes miracles ambiguous. Reflect: is there an aspect of your faith where you are relying on your own limited understanding rather than the clear proofs Allah has provided? What step will you take today to strengthen your certainty in prophethood?
?
When you hear stories about jinn affecting people's lives, do you automatically attribute them to jinn, or do you recall that miracles are signs from Allah given exclusively to prophets? How can you train your heart to always refer miraculous events back to Allah rather than to created beings?

The Objection: Could Jinn Bear the Defamation?
Presents the objection: the argument is weak because a deceiver would bear defamation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the argument against jinn is weak because one who seeks misguidance would willingly bear defamation.

Core Idea
Opponents argue that a deceiver would endure curses to mislead people.

This lesson covers the objection raised against the third argument. The opponent claims that the argument is weak because nothing prevents someone who wants to misguide people from bearing the defamation and curses that come with being labeled a false prophet. The author does not provide an immediate response in this text.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text of the objection and its translation. Notice how the opponent directly attacks the third argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says the argument is 'weak speech' (kalam da'if) in repelling a 'strong difficulty' (ishkal qawi).
3
Understand the reasoning
The opponent argues that a deceiver (one who desires misguidance and misdirection) would not mind bearing defamation and curses to achieve his goal.
4
Consider the implications
This objection suggests that even if miracles could be attributed to jinn, a false prophet might still succeed by enduring criticism. The author may address this later.

Fourth Proof: Magicians' Poverty Refutes Jinn Assistance
Presents the fourth proof: practitioners of magic suffer poverty, showing jinn do not help them.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
If jinn truly aided magicians, they would not be poor and humiliated.

This lesson presents the fourth argument against the existence of helpful jinn. Instead of a theoretical proof, it points to observable reality: those who claim to have power over jinn—magicians and astrologers—live in poverty and humiliation. If jinn were real and supportive, they would surely benefit their followers. The fact that they do not undermines the claim.

4

Refuting Jinn as Subtle Bodies
Presents second part of first argument against jinn existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that jinn cannot be subtle bodies and how this refutation supports the overall argument against corporeal jinn.

Core Idea
Corporeal jinn would be too fragile or too weak to match their legendary feats.

This lesson continues the first argument against jinn existence by examining the possibility that they are subtle bodies. The author offers two clear reasons why this is impossible, leading to the conclusion that any physicalist account of jinn is incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
The passage is a concise argument with two parts.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two specific reasons given why jinn cannot be subtle bodies. Note how each is presented.
The reasons are introduced by 'فذلك' and 'وأيضاً'.
3
Examine the conclusion
Observe how the author ties the two reasons together and concludes that the assertion of jinn is false. What is the implied contrast with the earlier part?
The conclusion references 'فساد القسمين'—both categories (dense and subtle) have been refuted.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why the author considers these reasons sufficient. What assumptions does the argument rely on?
The argument assumes that reported abilities of jinn are accurate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها من الأجسام اللطيفة . فذلك لأنها لو كانت كذلك ، لتفرقت وتمزقت عند هبوب الرياح القوية العاصفة ، فكان يجب أن تموت بأسرع الأسباب ، وأقل الموجبات. وأيضاً : فالأجسام اللطيفة لا تكون قادرة على الأفعال الشاقة. والناس يصفونها بالقدرة على الأعمال الشاقة . ولما ثبت فساد القسمين ، ظهر أن القول بإثبات الجن والشياطين : باطل .
And as for the explanation that it is impossible for them to be of the subtle bodies: that is because if they were so, they would disperse and tear apart at the blowing of the strong violent winds, and thus they would necessarily die from the fastest causes and the least occasions. Also: subtle bodies are not capable of strenuous actions. Yet people describe them as capable of strenuous actions. And when the corruption of both categories has been established, it becomes clear that the assertion of the existence of jinn and devils is false.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is the second part of the first argument against the existence of jinn and shayāṭīn. The author argues that jinn cannot be subtle bodies (al-ajsām al-laṭīfa). First, if they were subtle, they would be scattered by strong winds and die quickly. Second, subtle bodies cannot perform difficult tasks, yet people attribute such abilities to jinn. Since both possibilities (dense or subtle) have been refuted (the first part regarding dense bodies is assumed earlier), the claim that jinn exist is invalid.

Key Terms
الأجسام اللطيفة
الأجسام الكثيفة
الجن
الشياطين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves jinn do not exist at all.
Correction: The argument only refutes a specific physicalist conception (dense or subtle bodies). It does not address non-corporeal existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given that jinn cannot be subtle bodies?
A) They would be invisible.
B) They would be destroyed by strong winds and unable to perform heavy tasks.
C) They would be immortal.
D) They would be omniscient.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that a physicalist (corporeal) understanding of jinn is self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses logical reasoning to reject a physical understanding of jinn. In your own life, do you ever reduce spiritual realities (like faith, prayer, or the unseen) to mere physical or material terms? How might that distort your relationship with the divine?
?
If jinn are not made of matter, then the unseen world operates on entirely different principles. Does your daily life reflect a genuine belief in a reality beyond the physical, or do you live as if only what you can see and touch matters?
?
The passage shows that even our reported experiences of the unseen must be consistent with reason. Are you willing to let your intellect correct your comfortable but flawed beliefs about God, the unseen, or your own faith, even when it's uncomfortable?

The Argument from the Poverty of Magicians
Fourth argument: if jinn existed, their allies would be wealthy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable poverty of magicians is used as a proof against the existence of jinn.

Core Idea
If jinn helped their allies, magicians would be rich; they are not, so jinn do not exist.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from al-Matalib al-'Aliyah, which relies on empirical observation. The author notes that practitioners of incantations and astrology are often poor and miserable. If jinn truly supported them, we would expect these magicians to be prosperous and honored. Since they are not, the claim that jinn exist and interact with humans is baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the observation
The author starts with an observation: magicians are poor, hungry, naked, humiliated, and wretched. This is the factual premise.
3
Understand the conditional claim
The author then states a conditional: if the stories about jinn were true, jinn would favor their allies (the magicians) with bounty and honor.
This conditional links the belief in jinn to expected worldly outcomes.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since the expected outcome (wealth and honor) is not observed, the author concludes that the stories have no basis.
This is a logical inference: observation contradicts expectation, so the premise is false.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether this empirical argument is convincing. What assumptions does it make about jinn and their behavior?
This connects to the common misunderstanding mentioned in the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة (١) : إنا نرى أرباب العزائم والتنجيمات واقعين في الفقر والجوع والعري ، والذلة والمسكنة. ولو كان لهذا الحديث أصل ، لكان الظاهر من حال أولئك الجن أن يخصوا أنصارهم وأحباءهم - وهم هؤلاء المعزمون - بنوع من النعمة والكرامة . ولما لم نر شيئاً من ذلك ، علمنا : أنه لا أصل لهذا الحديث .
The fourth proof: We see the masters of incantations and astrological practices falling into poverty, hunger, nakedness, humiliation, and wretchedness. And if this tradition had any basis, it would be apparent from the state of those jinn that they would single out their supporters and lovers—and they are these incanters—with some kind of bounty and honor. And since we do not see anything of that, we know that there is no basis for this tradition.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. The author observes that those who practice incantations (ʿazāʾim) and astrology (tanjīmāt) are often poor and miserable. If jinn truly helped their allies, these practitioners would be wealthy and honored. Since they are not, the belief that jinn exist and aid people is baseless.

Key Terms
العزائم
التنجيمات
الجن
النعمة
الكرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes jinn are supposed to reward their followers materially, but perhaps jinn's help is not necessarily material or visible.
Correction: The author's argument relies on the assumption that if jinn existed and had relationships with humans, they would manifest in observable material benefits. However, a defender of jinn existence could claim that jinn's assistance is spiritual, hidden, or not bound to worldly prosperity. The argument is only as strong as that assumption.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author present to disprove the existence of jinn in this argument?
A) Magicians are wealthy.
B) Magicians are poor and miserable despite their claimed alliance with jinn.
C) Jinn are invisible.
D) Jinn are kind to everyone.

Key Takeaway
The observable poverty of magicians is used as a logical disproof of jinn existence, assuming jinn would materially reward their allies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a story or belief about the unseen that you have accepted without questioning its observable consequences. Where does that belief lead when tested by the logic of this passage? What would you need to see to be convinced it is true?
?
The magicians' poverty exposed their claim. Is there an area of your life where your actions or claims are contradicted by observable reality? What would it cost you to admit that evidence?
?
This argument assumes that if jinn existed, they would act in a predictable way (rewarding allies). Could your own assumptions about how God or the unseen should behave be silencing evidence that challenges your faith? How do you balance trust in tradition with the demand for evidence?

The Argument from Lack of Rational Claimants
Fifth argument: only fools claim contact with jinn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth argument uses a logical dichotomy to refute claims of jinn contact based on the absence of rational claimants.

Core Idea
No rational claimant = no rational jinn.

This passage presents the fifth argument against the existence of jinn. It uses a clever dichotomy: either jinn are mindless or intelligent. If mindless, connecting with them is pointless. If intelligent, they would prefer the company of the wise. Yet only fools report such encounters. This lesson unpacks why this argument is compelling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure.
The author uses a two-part conditional to set up the dichotomy.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Pinpoint the two cases: jinn being non-intelligent vs. intelligent. For each, consider the implication drawn by the author.
The conclusion from the first case is that seeking them is futile; from the second, they'd prefer associating with rational humans.
3
Observe the empirical claim
The author states that no rational, verified person claims jinn appearance; only the insane and foolish do. Ask yourself why this is significant.
This observation is the key evidence that leads to the final conclusion.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that such claims are either lies promoted to simpletons or defects in imagination. Connect this back to the dichotomy.
The conclusion directly attacks the credibility of all reported jinn contacts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن هؤلاء الجن إن كانوا غير موصوفين بشيء من العقل . فلا فائدة في التقرب إليهم، وفي طلب الاتصال بهم . . لأن المجنون المعتوه لا اعتماد على صداقته ولا على عداوته . وإن كانوا موصوفين بالعقل ، والفهم والإدراك ، وجب أن يكونوا باحثين عن مسائل الإلهيات والطبيعيات متفكرين في الدلائل [والشبهات (۱) ] وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن [ يكون (۲) ] ميلهم إلى المخالطة [ بالعقلاء الأذكياء من البشر أكثر من ميلهم إلى المخالطة (۳) ] بالجهال والأعمار. ولكنا لا نرى أحداً من العقلاء المحقين المحققين يدعي ظهور الجن له البتة . وإنما يدعي هذه الواقعة : المجانين والأعمار من الناس ) وذلك يدل على أن مدار هذا الباب ، إما على ترويج الأكاذيب على الأغمار ، وإما على الخلل والحيل في الخيالات والأفكار (٤) ] .
The fifth proof: If these jinn are not described with any intelligence, then there is no benefit in drawing near to them or seeking connection with them, because the insane fool, neither his friendship nor his enmity can be trusted. And if they are described with intelligence, understanding, and perception, then it would be necessary that they investigate matters of theology and natural science and reflect on proofs and doubts. And if that is so, then it would be necessary that their inclination to associate with intelligent, clever humans be greater than their inclination to associate with the ignorant and fools. But we do not see any of the rational, verified, verifying people claiming that jinn appear to them at all; rather, only the insane and the foolish people claim this occurrence. And that indicates that the basis of this matter is either the promotion of lies to the simpletons, or defects and tricks in imaginations and thoughts.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against the existence of jinn. The author argues from a dichotomy: either jinn are mindless or intelligent. If mindless, associating with them is useless. If intelligent, they would prefer the company of rational and learned humans over ignorant ones. Yet, no truly rational person claims to have seen jinn; only the insane and foolish do. Therefore, claims of jinn contact are either lies or delusions.

Key Terms
العقل
المجانين
الأغمار
العقلاء
الخيالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that because many individuals claim to have seen jinn, jinn must exist.
Correction: This argument points out that the claimants are exclusively irrational, so their testimony is unreliable. It does not prove jinn don't exist, but it undermines the evidence from reported contact.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what does the fact that only fools claim jinn contact indicate?
A) Jinn are only interested in fools.
B) The claims are either lies or delusions.
C) Jinn are actually very intelligent.
D) The prophets also saw jinn.

Key Takeaway
This argument shows that claims of jinn interaction are only made by irrational people, undermining their credibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only the insane and foolish claim jinn contact, what does your own fascination with such stories say about your spiritual discernment? Will you commit to testing all claims about the unseen against reason and revelation?
?
This argument uses a logical dichotomy to dismantle false claims. Are you willing to apply the same rigorous thinking to your own beliefs, even when they are emotionally comforting? What is one belief you hold that you have not critically examined?
?
The passage concludes that such claims are either lies or delusions. Have you ever spread a story about the unseen without verifying its truth? How will you guard your tongue and your heart from spreading falsehood?

The Fifth Argument Against Jinn: A Rational Dilemma
Presents a dilemma argument against the existence of jinn based on rationality and observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of a dilemma argument against the existence of jinn based on intellect and observation.

Core Idea
A rational dilemma: if jinn are rational, they should seek the rational; only the insane claim contact.

This passage presents the fifth argument against the existence of jinn, structured as a dilemma. The argument challenges the rationality of belief in jinn by examining whether they possess intellect. By following the logical steps, you will see how the argument uses both reason and empirical observation to cast doubt on jinn phenomena. However, note that this argument is later refuted in the same work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Try to follow the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities: jinn have no intellect, or they have intellect. Notice how each leads to a different conclusion about human interaction.
The first horn leads to uselessness; the second horn leads to expectation of rational association.
3
Analyze the observational evidence
The argument claims that no rational person reports jinn sightings; only the insane and gullible do. Consider how this inductive observation supports the dilemma's conclusion.
This step connects the logical reasoning with empirical evidence.
4
Understand the final conclusion
The conclusion is that jinn phenomena are either deliberate lies targeting the gullible or products of mental disturbance. Reflect on how this conclusion follows from the dilemma and observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن هؤلاء الجن إن كانوا غير موصوفين بشيء من العقل ألبتة . فلا فائدة في التقرب إليهم، وفي طلب الاتصال بهم . لأن المجنون المعتوه لا اعتماد على صداقته ولا على عداوته . وإن كانوا موصوفين بالعقل ، والفهم والإدراك ، وجب أن يكونوا باحثين عن مسائل الإلهيات والطبيعيات متفكرين في الدلائل والشبهات وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون ميلهم إلى المخالطة بالعقلاء الأذكياء من البشر أكثر من ميلهم إلى المخالطة بالجهال والأغمار. لكنا لا نرى أحداً من العقلاء المحقين المحققين يدعي ظهور الجن له البتة . وإنما يدعي هذه الواقعة : المجانين والأغمار من الناس . وذلك يدل على أن مدار هذا الباب ، إما على ترويج الأكاذيب على الأغمار ، وإما على الخلل والحيل في الخيالات والأفكار . فهذا مجموع ما يمكن أن يقال في إنكار الجن والشياطين .
Fifth argument: If these jinn are not described with any intellect at all, then there is no benefit in drawing near to them, nor in seeking connection with them. For the insane idiot cannot be relied upon for his friendship nor his enmity. And if they are described with intellect, understanding, and perception, then they must be researchers into matters of theology and natural sciences, reflecting on proofs and doubts. And if that is the case, then their inclination to associate with rational intelligent humans must be greater than their inclination to associate with the ignorant and the gullible. Yet we do not see any of the rational, truthful, and verifiers claim the appearance of jinn to them at all. Rather, only the insane and the gullible among people claim this occurrence. And that indicates that the basis of this matter is either the promotion of lies among the gullible, or the disturbance and trickery in fantasies and thoughts. So this is the sum of what can be said in denial of jinn and devils.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument (proof) against the existence of jinn. It begins by stating the fifth argument (al-hujja al-khamisa). The argument is structured as a dilemma: either jinn possess intellect or they do not. If they lack intellect, then associating with them is pointless because an insane person's friendship or enmity is unreliable. If they possess intellect, then they would naturally be inclined to seek out intelligent and rational humans for association, and would engage in intellectual pursuits. But observation shows that no rational, truthful, and verified person ever claims to have seen a jinn; only the insane and gullible make such claims. Therefore, the entire phenomenon is either a deliberate propagation of lies directed at the gullible, or a product of mental disturbances and fantasies. The passage concludes by stating that this is the sum of what can be said in denial of jinn and devils.

Key Terms
جن (jinn)
عقل (intellect)
مجنون (insane)
أغمار (gullible)
استقراء (induction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is a definitive proof that jinn do not exist.
Correction: This argument is presented as one among several; the author later refutes it, showing it is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, why is it unlikely that jinn exist if they are rational?
A) They would hide from humans out of fear.
B) They would seek to associate with intelligent humans, yet only the insane claim contact.
C) They would be too busy with their own affairs.
D) They would be invisible and thus never seen.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses a dilemma and inductive observation to argue that jinn phenomena are either lies or mental disturbances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in the unseen—does your belief rest on rational conviction or on stories you've heard? Which horn of this dilemma would you rather be in, and why?
?
If you have never personally encountered a jinn, does that silence shake your certainty? Or do you rely on revelation alone? How does this argument push you to clarify your basis for faith?
?
Consider: are you among the 'rational truthful verifiers' who bases faith on evidence, or have you accepted claims about the unseen without scrutiny? What would it cost you to examine them?

Response to the First Objection: Jinn as Non-Bodily Substance
Refutes the assumption that jinn must be bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that jinn must be bodies by proposing they could be self-subsistent substances.

Core Idea
Jinn may be non-bodily self-subsistent substances, not bodies.

In this passage, the author directly addresses an objection that assumes jinn must be either dense or subtle bodies. He challenges the underlying premise that existence requires bodiliness and offers an alternative: jinn could be self-subsistent substances (jawhar mujarrad) that are neither bodies nor accidents. This response shifts the burden of proof onto the objector.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If ... then ...'.
2
Identify the objection's premise and challenge
The objection claims jinn must be bodies. Find where the author challenges this assumption and asks 'Why?'
The author questions the necessity of bodiliness.
3
Understand the alternative proposal
The author proposes jinn could be self-subsistent substances (jawhar mujarrad). Consider what this means and how it refutes the objection.
This alternative is neither body nor accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الأولى : أن يقال : إن قولكم : لو كانت الجن والشياطين موجودة لكانت إما أن تكون جسماً كثيفاً أو جسماً لطيفاً : مفرع على أنه لو كان موجوداً لكان جسماً . فلم قلتم : إن الأمر كذلك ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يكون جوهراً مجرداً قائماً بذاته ، وليس بجسم ولا حال في جسم ؟ فإنكم ما لم تقيموا الدلالة على فساد هذا القسم ، لم يتم كلامكم .
And the answer to the first argument is that it is said: Your saying, 'If jinn and devils existed, they would either be a dense body or a subtle body' is based on the assumption that if it existed, it would be a body. So why did you say that? And why is it not permissible that it be a self-subsistent substance, not a body nor an accident in a body? For as long as you have not established the proof of the invalidity of this division, your statement is not complete.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage gives the answer to the first objection (al-hujja al-ula) raised against the existence of jinn. The objection claimed that if jinn exist, they must be either dense or subtle bodies. The response challenges the premise that jinn must be bodies at all. It points out that the objection assumes that any existent being must be a body. The answer then proposes that jinn could instead be a self-subsistent substance (jawhar mujarrad qā'im bi-dhātih) that is neither a body nor an accident (ḥāl) in a body. The response argues that until the opponent proves that a non-bodily substance is impossible, their argument remains incomplete.

Key Terms
جوهر مجرد (self-subsistent substance)
جسم (body)
حال (accident)
دلالة (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'self-subsistent substance' implies a Platonic form or something like a soul-substance separate from body.
Correction: In kalam, 'jawhar mujarrad' often refers to an independent non-material entity, such as an angel or jinn, that is not a body nor an accident. It does not necessarily imply a Platonic idea.

Checkpoint
What does the author suggest as an alternative to the claim that jinn must be bodies?
A) They are purely spiritual without any substance.
B) They are self-subsistent substances (jawhar mujarrad) that are neither bodies nor accidents.
C) They are dense bodies but invisible.
D) They are illusions with no reality.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by proposing that jinn could be non-bodily self-subsistent substances, thereby undermining the assumption that existence requires bodiliness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made an assumption about the unseen world that you later realized was based on incomplete reasoning? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink your approach to matters of the unseen?
?
The author insists that the objector must prove the impossibility of non-bodily substances before his argument can stand. In your own life, are there beliefs about Allah or the unseen that you hold without ever questioning their underlying assumptions? Will you today commit to examining one such assumption?

Responding to the Objection of Dense Body Visibility
Author defers answer to a prior detailed work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to the objection that jinn could be dense bodies, and why he refers to another book.

Core Idea
A strong objection may require a separate, deeper investigation.

This passage presents the second objection against jinn being dense bodies. The opponent argues that if jinn were dense, everyone with sound senses would see them. The author's response is brief but significant: he acknowledges the strength of the issue and directs the reader to a dedicated treatment elsewhere. This teaches us a valuable method in kalam: when an objection is genuinely strong, it may require a separate investigation rather than a quick answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, opponent's premise, author's reply.
The passage is short; read it carefully.
2
Identify the objection and reasoning
The opponent concedes jinn are bodies but asks why not dense. He argues that if dense, everyone would see them. Find this in the text.
The opponent's premise is 'لو كان كذلك، لوجب أن يراه كل أحد'.
3
Observe the author's response
The author says the issue is strong and refers to his book 'Al-Hiss wa al-Mahsus'. Notice he does not give a direct answer here.
This is a deferral, not an evasion.
4
Reflect on the scholarly method
Think about why a scholar might defer an answer. What does this tell you about the nature of kalam and scholarly inquiry?
Deeper, specialized works handle complex issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أنه جسم . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً كثيفاً ؟ قوله : لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن يراه كل أحد ممن له حس سليم . قلنا : هذه المسألة قوية ، سبق الاستقصاء فيها في كتاب الحس والمحسوس .
The second question: We grant that it is a body. So why is it not permissible that it be a dense body? His saying: If it were so, it would be necessary that everyone with sound senses would see it. We say: This issue is strong; the investigation into it has preceded in the book 'Al-Hiss wa al-Mahsus' (Sense and the Sensible).
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a response to the second objection (al-su'āl al-thānī). The opponent concedes (sallamnā) that jinn are bodies but asks why they cannot be dense bodies (jism kathīf). The objection is that if jinn were dense, they would be visible to anyone with sound senses, but they are not seen. The answer is very brief: the author says this is a strong issue (mas'ala qawiyya) and that a full investigation has already been done in his book 'Al-Hiss wa al-Mahsus' (The Sense and the Sensible). Thus, the response defers to a previous detailed discussion rather than repeating it.

Key Terms
جسم كثيف
حس سليم
الحس والمحسوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is evading the question or giving a weak answer.
Correction: In kalam, deferring to a dedicated work is a valid scholarly method, not an evasion. It shows the objection is taken seriously.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that a dense jinn would be universally visible?
A) He provides a new argument proving jinn are not dense.
B) He says the issue is complex and refers to his book 'Al-Hiss wa al-Mahsus'.
C) He denies that jinn are bodies altogether.
D) He says that only prophets can see jinn.

Key Takeaway
A strong objection does not always receive an immediate full answer; sometimes the scholar acknowledges its weight and directs to a more detailed investigation elsewhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult question about your faith, do you have the humility to admit it is strong and seek deeper knowledge, or do you give superficial answers? This lesson shows the author acknowledging the strength of the objection and referring to a specialized work. What strong question have you been avoiding, and what step will you take today to find the answer?
?
The opponent conceded a point but pushed further. In your own learning, do you stop at surface-level acceptance, or do you push deeper until you understand the 'why'? This lesson challenges you not to be content with easy answers. What is one belief you hold that you have not thoroughly investigated, and how can you pursue its deeper proof?
?
The author did not brush aside the objection; he recognized its strength and directed to a prior work. Are you honest about the difficulties in your beliefs, or do you ignore them? This lesson calls you to face strong questions and seek out the comprehensive answers scholars have already given. What question will you commit to exploring this week, and what source will you consult?

Three Possibilities for Subtle Jinn
Three possibilities for subtle jinn's persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three possible explanations given by theologians to defend the existence of subtle jinn against the objection that they would disintegrate in strong winds.

Core Idea
Subtle bodies need not disintegrate if they are solid, soul-like, or have an indivisible core.

This passage answers a key objection to the reality of jinn: if they are subtle bodies, why don't they tear apart? The response offers three distinct possibilities, each drawing on different theological conceptions of body and soul. Understanding these shows the flexibility of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and possibilities
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then three possible answers (iḥtimālāt).
The passage is a direct response to a critical doubt.
2
Understand the first possibility
The first possibility says jinn are transparent and colorless but solid and strongly composed, so they don't disintegrate. Consider how this distinguishes subtlety from fragility.
Solidity here preserves form despite subtlety.
3
Analyze the second possibility
The second draws an analogy with humans: either humans are essentially souls (so jinn can be similar) or humans are bodies preserved inside a decaying body (so jinn can persist likewise). Reflect on the logic of analogy.
This shifts the burden to the opponent's own assumptions.
4
Examine the third possibility
The third cites the view that man is an indivisible part in the heart, with other parts as instruments. This core persists. Apply the same to jinn.
This appeals to an established theological position.
5
Synthesize the response
Notice how each possibility independently refutes the objection without contradiction. The multiplicity shows the strength of the theological defense.
The objection fails unless all possibilities are false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أنه جسم . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً لطيفاً ؟
قوله : لو كان كذلك ، لتمزق وتفرق عند هبوب الرياح الشديدة .
قلنا : ههنا احتمالات :
أحدها: لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنها لطيفة . بمعنى أنها شفافة غير ملونة ؟ فلا جرم لا تجب رؤيتها عند الحضور، إلا أنها صلبة في ذواتها قوية في تركيبها ، فلا جرم تبقى مصونة عن التفرق والتمزق .
وثانيها : إن الإنسان إما أن يكون عبارة عن النفس ، أو عن الجسم .
فإن كان الأول فجوزوا مثله في الجن وسقطت هذه الشبهة . وإن كان الثاني : فحينئذ يجب أن يقال : إنه جسم محفوظ باق من أول العمر إلى آخره ، في داخل هذا البدن المتفرق المتحلل . وبالطريق الذي عقلتم ذلك ، فاعقلوا مثله في بقاء الجن والشياطين مدة طويلة ، مع كونها كثيفة .
وثالثها : أليس أن من المتكلمين من قال : الإنسان جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب، وأما سائر أجزاء البدن ، فهي كالآلات ، وعلى تقدير أن يكون الإنسان جسماً ، فهذا القول أقوى الأقوال ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في الجن والشياطين كذلك ؟ .
The third question: We grant that it is a body. So why is it not permissible that it be a subtle body? His saying: If it were so, it would be torn apart and disintegrate at the time of strong winds. We say: Here are possibilities: First: Why is it not permissible to say that they are subtle, meaning that they are transparent and colorless? Then necessarily their sight is not required when present, except that they are solid in their essences, strong in their composition, so necessarily they remain safeguarded from disintegration and tearing. Second: Man is either the soul or the body. If the former, then allow the same for the jinn and this doubt falls. And if the latter, then in that case it must be said that he is a preserved body remaining from the beginning of life to its end, inside this decaying, dissolving body. And by the same way you understood that, understand the like regarding the permanence of jinn and devils for a long time, even if they are dense. Third: Is it not that among the mutakallimun there is one who said: Man is an indivisible part in the heart, and the rest of the body parts are like instruments. And assuming that man is a body, this saying is the strongest of sayings. So why is it not permissible that the case with jinn and devils be similarly?
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a response to the third objection (al-su'āl al-thālith). The opponent concedes that jinn are bodies but asks why they cannot be subtle bodies (jism laṭīf). The objection is that subtle bodies would be torn apart by strong winds. The answer presents three possibilities (iḥtimālāt). First: Jinn may be subtle in the sense of being transparent and colorless, yet solid and strongly composed, so they do not disintegrate. Second: Comparing jinn to humans: if humans are essentially souls, then jinn can be similar; if humans are bodies, then they remain preserved inside a decaying body—likewise jinn can remain long despite being dense. Third: Some theologians say man is an indivisible part in the heart, with other body parts as instruments; similarly jinn could have such a core that persists. Each possibility blocks the objection that subtle bodies must disintegrate.

Key Terms
جسم لطيف
شفافة غير ملونة
نفس
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third possibility is a minority opinion and not an established position in kalam.
Correction: The third possibility refers to the atomistic view of the human soul, which is an established kalam position held by many mutakallimun.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the possibilities given for how subtle jinn avoid disintegration?
A) They are transparent and colorless but solid.
B) They are like the human soul, which persists.
C) They become invisible when wind blows.
D) They are like the indivisible part in the heart according to some theologians.

Key Takeaway
Theologians offered at least three plausible explanations for how subtle bodies like jinn can persist without disintegrating, demonstrating the sophistication of kalam arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun used reason to defend the existence and persistence of jinn. When you encounter doubts about unseen matters from modern science or skepticism, do you trust that theological reasoning can be sufficient? What does your answer reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
The second possibility draws an analogy between humans and jinn. Reflect: Do you treat the unseen world as real and parallel to your own existence, or do you subconsciously relegate it to myth? Tonight, before sleeping, recite the ayat about the unseen and ask yourself if you truly live as if it is as certain as what you see.
?
If you were challenged to defend the existence of jinn to a skeptic, which of the three possibilities would you use? Why? What does your choice say about how you rely on rational arguments versus revealed texts?

Responses to the Second and Third Doubts
Answers doubts about jinn's power and starts a counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that jinn's power over human bodies is conditional, and that the response to the third doubt begins with a counter-argument.

Core Idea
Jinn's power is not absolute; it depends on specific conditions.

The doubts about jinn's power often assume that if they can perform difficult deeds, they must also be able to affect humans. Sanusi clarifies that power has levels, and affecting human bodies requires a special condition. The answer to the third doubt starts a counter-argument, but the text breaks off, leaving the full rebuttal unfinished.

Read the Arabic passage

The third answer is incomplete on this page.

The phrase 'illa bi-sharṭ khāṣṣ' (except with a specific condition) is crucial.
3
Reflect on the third answer's beginning
Note that the response to the third doubt starts with 'What you have mentioned is contradicted by another aspect.' This signals a rebuttal by showing a flaw in the opponent's reasoning.
The full argument is not available in this window.
4
Consider the broader implication
Ask yourself: why is it important to say that power levels differ? This prevents over- or under-estimating jinn's capabilities.

This passage contains the answers to two doubts (shubah) raised against the existence or efficacy of jinn. The second doubt likely questioned how jinn could have power but not affect humans. The answer is that levels of power (marātib al-qudra) vary: jinn may be capable of strenuous deeds but lack the ability to affect human bodies unless a specific condition is present; when that condition is absent, the power is not actualized. Why Jinn Are Not Dense Bodies
The author addresses the objection that jinn could be dense bodies and refers to prior discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author does not directly refute the objection about jinn being dense bodies but instead references earlier theological discussions.

Core Idea
Some objections are answered by pointing to prior established scholarship.

In this brief exchange, the author faces a second objection: if jinn are bodies, why can't they be dense like us? The objector argues that dense bodies are visible to everyone with sound senses. Instead of crafting a new argument, the author acknowledges the question's strength and directs the reader to a separate book where the issue has already been thoroughly investigated. This teaches us that systematic theology often builds on prior discussions, and not every objection requires a novel response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector concedes jinn are bodies but asks why not dense. Note the logic: if dense, then visible.
The objector uses a conditional: if jinn were dense, they would be seen.
2
Examine the author's response
The author calls the question strong, then refers to his previous work 'Al-Hiss wa al-Mahsus'.
The response is not a direct refutation but a referral.
3
Reflect on the method
Why would the author refer elsewhere? Consider the role of prior scholarship in Islamic theology.
This shows that theological arguments are cumulative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أنه جسم . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً كثيفاً ؟ قوله : ( لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن يراه كل أحد ممن له حس سليم ، قلنا : هذه المسألة قوية ، سبق الاستقصاء فيها في كتاب ( الحس والمحسوس ) .
Second question: We concede that it is a body. So why is it not permissible that it be a dense body? His statement: 'If it were so, it would be necessary that everyone with sound senses would see it.' We said: This question is strong; a thorough investigation preceded it in the book 'Al-Hiss wa al-Mahsus'.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer in a kalam debate about the nature of jinn. The objector concedes that jinn are bodies but asks why they cannot be dense bodies. The objector's argument: if jinn were dense, they would be visible to everyone with sound senses. The author responds not by refuting the premise directly but by acknowledging the strength of the question and referring to a prior investigation in another book, implying that the matter has already been settled. This shows that in systematic theology, some objections are addressed by pointing to earlier established discussions.

Key Terms

حس سليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He refers to a prior discussion
C) He concedes the objection
D) He changes the topic

Key Takeaway
In theological debates, not every objection needs a new answer; sometimes the response is to refer to earlier established studies.

Three Possibilities Answering the Objection About Jinn's Subtle Bodies
The author offers three possibilities to answer the objection that jinn would disintegrate if light

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possibilities the author presents to counter the objection that subtle bodies would disintegrate in strong winds.

Core Idea
Jinn could be transparent yet solid, or analogous to human soul/body persistence.

The objection argues that if jinn were subtle bodies, they would tear apart in strong winds. The author responds not with a single answer but with three distinct possibilities, each offering a different way to conceive of jinn's physical nature while preserving their integrity. This passage showcases the use of analogical reasoning in kalam to defend theological positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the author's initial response
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the objection and the author's statement 'Here are probabilities.'
2
Identify and understand the first possibility
The first possibility: jinn are subtle in the sense of being transparent and uncolored, but solid and strong in composition, thus they do not disintegrate.
Notice the distinction between subtlety (transparency) and strength.
3
Analyze the second possibility
The second possibility draws an analogy with human persistence: either man is soul (then jinn are similar) or man is a preserved body inside a decaying one (then jinn can persist similarly).
This uses the principle of analogy (qiyas) from human nature to jinn.
4
Examine the third possibility
The third possibility references the view of some mutakallimun that man is an indivisible part in the heart, and the same could apply to jinn and devils.
This shows diversity of opinion within kalam and how it can be used to answer objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أنه جسم . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً لطيفاً ؟ قوله : ( لو كان كذلك ، لتمزق وتفرق عند هبوب الرياح الشديدة ) . قلنا : ههنا احتمالات : أحدها: لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنها لطيفة . بمعنى أنها شفافة غير ملونة ؟ فلا جرم لا تجب رؤيتها عند الحضور، إلا أنها صلبة في ذواتها قوية في تركيبها ، فلا جرم تبقى مصونة عن التفرق والتمزق . وثانيها : إن الإنسان. إما أن يكون عبارة عن النفس ، أو عن الجسم فإن كان الأول فجوزوا مثله في الجن وسقطت هذه الشبهة . وإن كان الثاني : فحينئذ يجب أن يقال : إنه جسم محفوظ باق من أول العمر إلى آخره ، في داخل هذا البدن المتفرق المتحلل . وبالطريق الذي عقلتم ذلك ، فاعقلوا مثله في بقاء الجن والشياطين مدة طويلة ، مع كونها كثيفة وثالثها : أليس أن من المتكلمين من قال : ( الإنسان جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب، وأما سائر أجزاء البدن ، فهي كالآلات ، وعلى تقدير أن يكون الإنسان جسماً ، فهذا القول أقوى الأقوال ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في الجن والشياطين ) كذلك ؟ .
Third question: We concede that it is a body. So why is it not permissible that it be a subtle body? His statement: 'If it were so, it would tear apart and scatter in strong winds.' We said: Here are probabilities: One: Why is it not permissible to say that they are subtle in the sense that they are transparent and uncolored? Thus their sight would not be obligatory when present, but they are solid in themselves and strong in composition, thus they remain free from scattering and tearing. And the second: That man is either an expression of the soul or of the body. If the former, then grant the like in the jinn, and this doubt falls. And if the latter, then it must be said that he is a preserved body remaining from the beginning of life to its end, inside this scattered decaying body. And by the method by which you understood that, understand likewise in the remaining of jinn and devils for a long time, even if they are dense. And the third: Does not one of the mutakallimun say that man is an indivisible part in the heart, and the other parts of the body are like instruments? And on the assumption that man is a body, this saying is the strongest of sayings, so why is it not permissible that the situation for jinn and devils is likewise?
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and a detailed answer with three possibilities. The objector argues that if jinn were subtle bodies, they would disintegrate in strong winds. The author responds by listing three possible explanations. First, jinn could be subtle in the sense of being transparent yet solid, so they are not seen but remain intact. Second, depending on whether man is defined as soul or body, jinn could be analogous to human persistence: if man is soul, then jinn can be similar; if man is a body, then the human body persists inside the decaying body, and jinn can persist similarly. Third, following the view of some mutakallimun that man is an indivisible part in the heart, one could say the same for jinn. This illustrates the use of analogical reasoning in kalam to counter an objection.

Key Terms
جسم لطيف
شفافة
صلبة
نفس
بدن
جزء لا يتجزأ
متكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If jinn are subtle bodies, they must be fragile and easily destroyed by wind.
Correction: Subtlety can refer to transparency, not weakness. Jinn could be solid and strong, or their persistence analogous to the human soul or an indivisible core.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author defends the possibility of jinn being subtle bodies by offering three analogical arguments: transparency with solidity, persistence like the soul, or an indivisible core.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What area of your faith do you doubt because you cannot perceive it, and how will you commit to trusting Allah's description over your senses starting today?
?

?
The third possibility mentions an indivisible core as the essence of a person. Do you view yourself as merely a physical body that decays, or do you acknowledge a permanent essence (like the soul) that remains? Answer to the second doubt: different levels of power
The author explains that jinn's power has conditions and does not extend to human bodies unconditionally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scope of jinn's power is qualified by conditions and levels, and how this refutes objections about their capability.

Core Idea
Power is conditional and graded, not absolute.

This passage addresses a doubt about the extent of jinn's power. The objection assumes that if jinn can perform difficult feats, they must also be able to affect human bodies. The author refutes this by introducing a key principle: power operates at different levels and depends on specific conditions. As you read, pay attention to how the author uses the concept of 'levels of power' to limit the jinn's domain without denying their ability in other areas.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: doubt, answer, explanation.
The passage is short but dense with theological nuance.
2
Identify the core argument
The author asserts that power has different levels (مراتب القدر). Find how this idea is used to answer the second doubt.
3
Understand the conditionality
The passage states that jinn have power over human bodies only 'except with a special condition'. Ask yourself: what would happen if that condition is absent?
This shows that power is not inherent but contingent.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This explanation preserves both the jinn's general capability and Allah's exclusive control over human bodies. Consider how this avoids overstepping the limits of created power.
This connects to the kalam principle of causality and interference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : فهو أن مراتب القدر مختلفة ، فلا يبعد أن يقال : إنهم وإن قدروا على الأفعال الشاقة ، إلا أنه لا قدرة لها على التصرف في أبدان البشر ، إلا بشرط خاص . وعند فقدان ذلك الشرط لا تحصل تلك القدرة .
As for the answer to the second doubt: It is that the levels of power are different, so it is not far-fetched to say that even if they are able to perform difficult actions, they have no power to act upon human bodies except with a special condition. And when that condition is absent, that power is not attained.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage answers a second doubt about jinn's power. The objector likely argued that since jinn can perform difficult actions, they should be able to affect human bodies. The author responds by noting that power has levels and conditions. Jinn may have power over some things but not over human bodies unless a specific condition is met. When that condition is absent, the power does not operate. This shows the kalam principle of distinguishing generic capability from conditioned capability, and it refutes the objection by qualifying the scope of jinn's power.

Key Terms
مراتب القدر
قدرة
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if jinn have power, they can do anything unconditionally.
Correction: The passage teaches that power is conditional and operates only when its specific conditions are met, so jinn cannot arbitrarily act on human bodies.

Checkpoint

B) They have power only under a special condition
C) They have no power at all
D) Their power is the same as humans

Key Takeaway
Power is not absolute; it varies in level and depends on conditions, which explains why jinn cannot arbitrarily affect human bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Prophetic Consensus on Jinn
The author argues that denying jinn implies denial of prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying the existence of jinn contradicts the unanimous testimony of the prophets, and thus implies a rejection of prophethood itself.

Core Idea
Rejecting jinn means rejecting the prophets who affirmed them.

The author addresses a third doubt about the existence of jinn by turning the argument around. He points out that all prophets have unanimously affirmed the reality of jinn and devils. Therefore, anyone who denies jinn must also reject the credibility of the prophets themselves, a position no Muslim can accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its English translation. Note the two parts: the claim that prophets agreed on jinn, and the consequence of denying them.
2
Understand the logical implication
Ask yourself: why does denying jinn force one to doubt the prophets? Because if the prophets were wrong about jinn, they could be wrong about other things, undermining their prophethood.
This is a 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether this argument is persuasive. The author relies on the unanimity of prophets. Is that a strong proof? The commentary will help you think about it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إن ما ذكرتموه معارض بوجه آخر . وهو أن الأنبياء عليهم السلام، أطبقوا على إثبات الجن والشياطين . فالطعن في عدمهم يوجب الطعن في نبوءة الأنبياء .
The answer to the third doubt: What you have mentioned is opposed by another aspect, which is that the prophets, peace be upon them, have unanimously affirmed the existence of jinn and devils. So casting doubt on their existence obligates casting doubt on the prophethood of the prophets.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage provides an argument from prophetic authority to answer a third doubt about jinn. The author states that the objector's doubt is countered by pointing to the unanimous affirmation of jinn and devils by all prophets. Therefore, denying the existence of jinn would undermine the credibility of the prophets themselves, because it would imply that they were mistaken or lying. This is a typical kalam argument using the consensus of prophets as a proof for a doctrinal truth. The consequence is clear: rejecting jinn leads to rejecting prophethood, which is unacceptable.

إثبات الجن

Misconception: One can accept the prophets while denying the existence of jinn.
Correction: Since the prophets unanimously affirmed jinn, denying jinn directly contradicts their testimony and thus undermines their prophethood.

Defusing a Dilemma: The Wonderful Method of Prophethood
Objection and answer about jinn and prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author handles an objection that claims both affirming and denying jinn cast doubt on prophethood, and why a proper response requires referencing a separate proof.

Core Idea
A dilemma is not a proof of weakness; the answer lies in its own chapter.

The author has just argued that affirming the existence of jinn implies casting doubt on prophethood. But an opponent quickly turns the tables: then denying jinn also casts doubt! This seems to create an inescapable dilemma. In this short but crucial passage, the author responds not by resolving the dilemma directly, but by pointing to a separate, independent proof for prophethood that lies beyond this discussion. This is a classic move in systematic kalam—showing that one topic's defense does not depend on another's resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent says: if affirming jinn leads to doubt, then denying them also leads to doubt. So the doubt is necessary either way. Read the Arabic and translation to see the exact phrasing.
Notice the phrase 'على كلا التقديرين' (on both estimates).
2
Identify the author's response
The author does not try to refute the objection directly. Instead, he says there is a 'wonderful method' for proving prophethood that will be mentioned later, and it is not subject to this objection.
The key phrase is 'طريق عجيب' (wonderful method).
3
Ask: why is this answer effective?
Consider that the objection is about jinn, but prophethood has its own independent proof. The author separates the two issues. This shows the systematic structure of kalam works.
This is a common pedagogical tactic: deferral to a later discussion.
4
Reflect on the overall strategy
The author does not abandon the argument; he places it in its proper context. This teaches us that objections can be answered by showing their limited scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إذا كان القول بوجودهم ، يوجب الطعن في نبوتهم ، فالقول بعدمهم يوجب الطعن أيضاً. فهذا الطعن لازم على كلا التقديرين . فنقول : لنا في إثبات النبوءة طريق عجيب تذكره في باب النبوات ، ولا يتوجه عليه مما ذكر لموه [؟] .

We say: We have in proving prophethood a wonderful method that will be mentioned in the chapter on prophethoods, and it is not subject to what was mentioned [؟].
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage records an objection from opponents and the author's response. The opponents argue that both affirming and denying the existence of jinn lead to doubt about prophethood: affirming jinn might imply that prophets spoke of beings that are not real (if jinn are considered problematic), and denying jinn contradicts prophetic statements. Thus, the doubt is inescapable. The author responds by postponing the full proof, stating that there is a special method for proving prophethood that will be discussed in its proper chapter, and that this method is not affected by the current objections. This demonstrates the structured nature of kalam works where topics are reserved for their proper place and shows confidence in the overall system.

Correction: The author is not avoiding the objection; he is showing that prophethood has its own independent proof that does not rely on the reality of jinn. This is a legitimate theological method.

The objection that both affirming and denying jinn cast doubt on prophethood is deflected by citing a separate, independent proof for prophethood that is discussed in its own chapter.

Dismissing Weak Rhetorical Arguments
The author dismisses the last two doubts as weak rhetorical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the category of weak rhetorical arguments and why the author entrusts them to the sound intellect rather than detailed refutation.

Core Idea
Not every objection deserves a detailed answer—some are simply weak.

The author concludes his responses to the series of doubts by addressing the last two. He does not provide a detailed rebuttal; instead, he categorizes them as 'weak rhetorical arguments' and appeals to the reader's own rational judgment. This lesson explores that dismissal and what it reveals about the role of reason in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the two sentences in Arabic. Notice the concise dismissal. The author uses the term 'إقناعيات ضعيفة' (weak rhetorical arguments).
This is a very brief passage, so read it slowly and note the key phrase.
2
Understand the category
Reflect on what 'weak rhetorical arguments' means. These are arguments that may sound persuasive but lack solid logical foundation. The author considers them unworthy of detailed refutation.
In kalam, arguments are classified by strength; some are merely rhetorical.
3
Appreciate the appeal to intellect
The author says 'anyone with sound intellect can investigate them'. This shows trust in the reader's rational ability to see the weakness. It also indicates that not every challenge demands a scholarly response.
This reflects a pedagogical principle: sometimes the best answer is to point out the weakness and let reason do the rest.

Commentary
This passage concludes the series of objections and answers by dismissing the last two doubts. The author categorizes them as weak rhetorical arguments (iqna'iyyat dha'ifah) and states that anyone with sound reason can look into them and see their weakness. This shows the author's judgment that not every challenge merits a detailed refutation; some are simply not persuasive and can be left to the reader's intellect. It also highlights the role of rational discernment in theological debate.

Evidence for Jinn: Consensus and Spiritual Experience
Author presents consensus and spiritual experience as evidence for jinn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses consensus and mystical experience as evidence for the existence of jinn, despite the lack of rational proof.

Core Idea
The existence of jinn is supported by consensus and spiritual experience, not rational proof.

The author acknowledges that there is no conclusive rational proof for the existence of jinn, but he presents compelling evidence from three groups: prophets, magicians, and mystics. This lesson examines that evidence and introduces a first argument from spiritual practice. Although the passage is incomplete, we can grasp the author's method of building a case from accepted premises.

The argument is incomplete, but the author poses a key question about the reality of these experiences.

The author poses the question: are these purely nothing, or are they real? The argument is cut off at this point.

Setting the Stage for the Argument
Razi introduces the argument and the three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi establishes the context for the first argument by noting the suspension of rational judgment and presenting the three possible explanatory categories for mystical experiences.

Core Idea
Mystical experiences during spiritual practices must be either nothing, mental, or real — only the third is plausible.

Razi begins by acknowledging that pure reason alone cannot prove or disprove jinn. Yet prophetic consensus, exorcist practices, and ascetic testimonies strongly suggest their reality. He then launches the first argument, starting with the observation that specific inner words and visions occur during solitude and spiritual exercises. These phenomena must fall into one of three categories: pure non-existence, mental existence, or external existence. The argument will proceed to eliminate the first two.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening context
Read from 'Know: We have shown...' up to the introduction of the three categories. Notice that Razi first establishes that no rational proof definitively affirms or denies jinn, but that prophetic and experiential evidence is strong.
This sets up the argument as addressing a matter of probability, not certainty from reason alone.
2
Identify the three categories
Locate the sentence where Razi enumerates the three possibilities: pure non-existence, mental existence, and external existence. Understand that these are the only logical options for the origin of inner words and visions.
3
Understand the argument's strategy
Recognize that the argument will now refute the first two categories to conclude that the third (external existence) is necessarily true, thereby proving jinn and devils are real entities that communicate with ascetics.
This lesson covers only the setup and classification; the refutations follow in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العالم [؟] السطورة الحالة على الكرات الجن والالي الحلوين ساس .
اعلم : أنا بينا : أنه لم يوجد دليل يدل على نفيهم وفساد القول بهم وظاهر أيضاً : أنه لم يوجد دليل عقلي يوجب الجزم بوجودهم . فيبقى الكلام فيهم في حيز التوقف. إلا أنا رأينا الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - مطبقين على إثباتهم ، وعلى القول بوجودهم - وذلك حجة قوية من المقدمات المقبولة - ورأينا المعزمين مطبقين متفقين على وجودهم ، حتى وضعوا لكل رئيس من رؤسائهم عزيمة معينة. وزعموا : أنهم ينتفعون بهم ، ويجدون منهم آثاراً صالحة . ورأينا أكثر الزهاد وأرباب المجاهدات والمكاشفات ، يزعمون : أنهم شاهدوا الجن ، وتكلموا معهم . فإن اتفق لإنسان أن يعتبر هذه الأحوال ويشاهد شيئاً منها ، فذاك هو البغية الأسنى ، والمقصد الأقصى . ومن الناس من يحتج عليهم بوجوه أخرى : الحجة الأولى : إن الإنسان إذا ألف الحلوة والوحدة ، واشتغل بالتصفية والرياضة ، وجد في نفسه ، كأن مناجياً يناجيه بكلمات مرتبة منظومة معلومة ، ورأى أشخاصاً موصوفة بصور مخصوصة وصفات مخصوصة . فنقول : هذه الكلمات وهذه الأشخاص. إما أن تكون عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً ، وإما أن تكون أموراً موجودة في الذهن والخيال ، وإما أن تكون أشياء موجودة في الأعيان كائنة في الجود . والقسمان الأولان باطلان ، فيبقى الثالث . وذلك هو الجن والشياطين . تناجيه وتناديه في سره وفي قلبه وباطنه . وإنما قلنا : إن القول بأن تلك الكلمات الواردة في الخواطر ، وأن تلك الصور المشاهدة في الباطن ، يمتنع أن تكون عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً . وذلك لأنا نسمع تلك الحروف من القلب والباطن ، ونفهم معانيها ، وتميز بينها وبين غيرها . فلو جاز القول بأنها مع اتصافها بهذه الصفات المعينة والخواص المعينة عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . لم يمتنع أيضاً في كون هذه الصور المخصوصة بهذه الحواس كونها عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً . وكل ذلك باطل محال . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنها موجودات في الأذهان ، لا في الأعيان . فهذا باطل قطعاً . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا لو جوزنا أن تكون هذه الألفاظ المترتبة المتعاقبة ، وأن تكون هذه الصور المخصوصة المتميزة عما يغايرها ، بألوانها وأشكالها : موجودات في الأذهان لا في الأعيان ، فلنجز في هذه الصور المحسوسة : كونها كذلك ويلزم منه السفسطة . والثاني : إن محل هذه الصور. إما أن يكون جسماً مخصوصاً كدماغ أو قلب ، وإما أن يكون جوهراً مجرداً ، والأول باطل . لأن الصور العظيمة يمتنع انطباعها في المحل الصغير. والثاني أيضاً باطل . لأن الجوهر المجرد لا يحصل فيه جوانب مختلفة ، ولا أجزاء متباينة . وإذا كان كذلك ، كانت الصورة ...
Know: We have shown that no evidence exists that proves their non-existence and the corruption of belief in them, and it is also apparent that no rational evidence necessitates certainty of their existence. So the discussion about them remains in the sphere of suspension. However, we find that the prophets (peace be upon them) are unanimous in affirming them and in asserting their existence—and that is a strong proof from accepted premises—and we find that the exorcists are unanimous and agreed upon their existence, such that they have appointed a specific exorcism for each of their chiefs. They claim that they benefit from them and find wholesome effects from them. And we find that most ascetics and practitioners of spiritual exertions and revelations claim that they witnessed the jinn and spoke with them. If a person happens to consider these states and witness something of them, that is the highest aspiration and the utmost goal. Among people are those who argue for them through other ways: The first argument: When a person becomes accustomed to solitude and seclusion, and occupies himself with purification and spiritual exercise, he finds within himself, as it were, an interlocutor addressing him with ordered, systematic, and known words, and he sees individuals described with specific forms and specific attributes. So we say: These words and these individuals are either pure non-existence and sheer nothingness, or they are things existing in the mind and imagination, or they are things existing externally in the world. The first two categories are invalid, so the third remains. That is the jinn and the devils. They converse with him and call him in his secret and in his heart and inner being. And we said: The claim that those words occurring in thoughts and those images witnessed inwardly cannot be pure non-existence and sheer nothingness. This is because we hear those letters from the heart and inner being, understand their meanings, and distinguish between them and others. If it were permissible to claim that they, despite being characterized by these specific attributes and specific properties, are pure non-existence and sheer nothingness, then it would not be impossible also that these specific images perceived by these senses are pure non-existence and sheer nothingness. All of that is false and impossible. As for the second category: that they are existents in minds, not externally. This is absolutely false. Two proofs indicate this: First: If we allowed that these ordered and successive utterances, and these specific images distinguished from what is other than them by their colors and shapes, are existents in minds not externally, then we should allow in these sensory images that they are likewise, and that would entail skepticism. Second: The locus of these images is either a specific body like the brain or heart, or an immaterial substance. The first is false, because large images cannot be impressed in a small locus. The second is also false, because an immaterial substance does not contain different sides or distinct parts. If that is the case, then the image...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the existence of jinn (جن) and devils (شياطين) based on the experiences of ascetics. It begins by acknowledging that no rational proof definitively establishes or refutes their existence, so the matter remains in suspension (توقف). However, the author notes strong evidence: the unanimous affirmation of prophets (إطباق الأنبياء), the consensus of exorcists (معزمين) who use specific incantations and report benefits, and the claims of ascetics (زهاد) who witness and speak with jinn. For those who still seek proof, the author presents the first argument: a person who practices solitude (خلوة), purification (تصفية), and spiritual exercise (رياضة) experiences inner words and visions. The source of these is classified into three categories: (1) pure non-existence (عدم محض), (2) mental existence (وجود في الذهن), or (3) external existence (وجود في الأعيان). The argument rejects the first two. For the first, if the words and images were nothing, then by analogy sensory objects could also be nothing, which is absurd. For the second, two refutations are given: (a) if these inner experiences are merely mental, then external sensory objects could also be claimed to be mental, leading to skepticism (سفسطة); (b) the locus of these images must be either a body (like brain/heart) or an immaterial substance. A bodily locus is impossible because large images cannot fit in a small space; an immaterial substance cannot contain differentiated parts, contradicting the structured nature of the images. Thus, the only valid category is external existence, confirming that jinn and devils are real entities that communicate with the seeker. The passage concludes mid-sentence, but the overall argument is complete in its structure.

Key Terms
العدم المحض (pure non-existence)
الوجود الذهني (mental existence)
الوجود العيني (external existence)
السفسطة (skepticism)
الجوهر المجرد (immaterial substance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the argument is based solely on personal experience without logical reasoning.
Correction: Razi does not rely on raw experience; he structures it into a logical trilemma and provides rational refutations for the alternatives.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first argument for jinn begins by classifying mystical experiences into three possible categories, setting up a logical elimination process.
