Refuting Non-Existence and Mental Existence
Razi refutes the first two categories of the trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the possibilities that inner experiences are pure non-existence or merely mental, thereby proving their external reality as jinn and devils.

Core Idea
If inner experiences were nothing or just mental, then all sensory experience would be equally doubtful — impossible.

In the previous lesson, we saw Razi classify inner experiences into three categories. Now he systematically eliminates the first two. First, he argues that if these specific words and images were pure non-existence, then by analogy all sensory objects could also be considered nothing, which is absurd. Second, he argues against mental existence with two proofs: it leads to skepticism (السفسطة) and it raises insurmountable difficulties regarding the locus of images. Thus, only external existence remains, confirming that jinn and devils are real entities that interact with ascetics.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of pure non-existence
Read the section starting with 'وإنما قلنا' to see why the experiences cannot be nothing. The key point: if such specific words and images were nothing, then sensory objects could also be nothing, which is absurd.
Note the use of analogy: if X (inner) were nothing, then Y (outer) could be nothing too.
2
Read the refutation of mental existence – first proof
The first proof against mental existence: if we allowed these inner words/images to be merely mental, then by the same reasoning we could claim that external sensory objects are merely mental, which would lead to skepticism (السفسطة).
Razi uses a 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.
3
Read the refutation of mental existence – second proof
The second proof focuses on the locus (محل) of the images. It must be either a body (like brain/heart) or an immaterial substance. A bodily locus is impossible because large images cannot fit in a small space. An immaterial substance is impossible because it cannot have parts or sides, yet the images have structure and differentiation.
This argument shows the incompatibility of mental existence with the nature of these experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Know: We have shown that no evidence exists that proves their non-existence and the corruption of belief in them, and it is also apparent that no rational evidence necessitates certainty of their existence. So the discussion about them remains in the sphere of suspension. However, we find that the prophets (peace be upon them) are unanimous in affirming them and in asserting their existence—and that is a strong proof from accepted premises—and we find that the exorcists are unanimous and agreed upon their existence, such that they have appointed a specific exorcism for each of their chiefs. They claim that they benefit from them and find wholesome effects from them. And we find that most ascetics and practitioners of spiritual exertions and revelations claim that they witnessed the jinn and spoke with them. If a person happens to consider these states and witness something of them, that is the highest aspiration and the utmost goal. Among people are those who argue for them through other ways: The first argument: When a person becomes accustomed to solitude and seclusion, and occupies himself with purification and spiritual exercise, he finds within himself, as it were, an interlocutor addressing him with ordered, systematic, and known words, and he sees individuals described with specific forms and specific attributes. So we say: These words and these individuals are either pure non-existence and sheer nothingness, or they are things existing in the mind and imagination, or they are things existing externally in the world. The first two categories are invalid, so the third remains. That is the jinn and the devils. They converse with him and call him in his secret and in his heart and inner being. And we said: The claim that those words occurring in thoughts and those images witnessed inwardly cannot be pure non-existence and sheer nothingness. This is because we hear those letters from the heart and inner being, understand their meanings, and distinguish between them and others. If it were permissible to claim that they, despite being characterized by these specific attributes and specific properties, are pure non-existence and sheer nothingness, then it would not be impossible also that these specific images perceived by these senses are pure non-existence and sheer nothingness. All of that is false and impossible. As for the second category: that they are existents in minds, not externally. This is absolutely false. Two proofs indicate this: First: If we allowed that these ordered and successive utterances, and these specific images distinguished from what is other than them by their colors and shapes, are existents in minds not externally, then we should allow in these sensory images that they are likewise, and that would entail skepticism. Second: The locus of these images is either a specific body like the brain or heart, or an immaterial substance. The first is false, because large images cannot be impressed in a small locus. The second is also false, because an immaterial substance does not contain different sides or distinct parts. If that is the case, then the image...

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the existence of jinn (جن) and devils (شياطين) based on the experiences of ascetics. It begins by acknowledging that no rational proof definitively establishes or refutes their existence, so the matter remains in suspension (توقف). However, the author notes strong evidence: the unanimous affirmation of prophets (إطباق الأنبياء), the consensus of exorcists (معزمين) who use specific incantations and report benefits, and the claims of ascetics (زهاد) who witness and speak with jinn. For those who still seek proof, the author presents the first argument: a person who practices solitude (خلوة), purification (تصفية), and spiritual exercise (رياضة) experiences inner words and visions. The source of these is classified into three categories: (1) pure non-existence (عدم محض), (2) mental existence (وجود في الذهن), or (3) external existence (وجود في الأعيان). The argument rejects the first two. For the first, if the words and images were nothing, then by analogy sensory objects could also be nothing, which is absurd. For the second, two refutations are given: (a) if these inner experiences are merely mental, then external sensory objects could also be claimed to be mental, leading to skepticism (سفسطة); (b) the locus of these images must be either a body (like brain/heart) or an immaterial substance. A bodily locus is impossible because large images cannot fit in a small space; an immaterial substance cannot contain differentiated parts, contradicting the structured nature of the images. Thus, the only valid category is external existence, confirming that jinn and devils are real entities that communicate with the seeker. The passage concludes mid-sentence, but the overall argument is complete in its structure.

الجوهر المجرد (immaterial substance)
محل (locus)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument proves that all mental images are externally real.
Correction: The argument only applies to specific experiences during spiritual exercises that have verifiable characteristics (ordered words, specific forms) and uses rational refutations to exclude non-existence and mental existence, leaving external existence as the only viable explanation.

Refutation of the Philosophers' Claim about the Thinking Power
The author refutes the philosophers' claim about the thinking power with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' notion of a separate thinking power is rationally untenable, and how the author identifies it with jinn.

Core Idea
The thinking power is not a human faculty but an independent spiritual entity—jinn—that acts against our will.

The philosophers object that the thinking power, which moves from one state to another, could be the agent of involuntary words. The author declares this false and offers two proofs. This passage demonstrates the rational method of refuting a philosophical objection through logical division and necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and dismissal
Read the philosophers' objection and the author's immediate declaration that it is absolutely false. Notice the confident tone.
The author does not engage with the objection directly yet; he first asserts falsehood.
2
Study the first proof
The first proof argues that the human is a unified agent of all perceptions and actions, so thought cannot be a separate doer. Follow the reasoning that the human did not intend these utterances, so they cannot be from him.
This proof relies on the principle that a single body must have a single agent.
3
Study the second proof
The second proof uses a tripartite division: the thinking power is either the human, an attribute, or an independent agent. The first two are eliminated, leaving an independent agent.
Pay attention to the elimination of 'attribute' because an attribute cannot act against the human's will.
4
Follow the deduction about the independent agent
The independent agent must be knowing and powerful, and cannot be an accident (since accidents cannot perform difficult actions). Therefore it is a self-subsistent essence.
Necessary knowledge (العلم الضروري) is used to rule out accidents.
5
Understand the identification and conclusion
The author identifies this essence as jinn and devils, saying the disagreement is merely verbal: the philosophers call it thinking power, while we call it jinn.
This reconciles the terminology and ends the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون فاعل تلك الكلمات ، وتلك الألفاظ : هو القوة المفكرة ، المنتقلة من صفة إلى صفة ، ومن حالة إلى حالة ؟ ونقول : هذا الكلام طال عهد الإنسان بسماعه ولهذا السبب تمكن في قلوبهم ) . وهو باطل قطعاً ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا بينا في المقالات المتقدمة : أن الموصوف بجميع الإدراكات [ والمتصرف ] بجميع أنواع التصرفات [ في هذا البدن يجب أن يكون شيئاً ] واحداً . وهو الإنسان . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يقال : الفكر عمل شيئاً آخر سوى الإنسان . لأنا قد دللنا على أن الإنسان لم يفعل هذه الألفاظ ، لأنه لا يريدها . بل يكرهها . فبطل هذا الكلام الثاني : إن هذا الذي سميتموه بالقوة المفكرة . إما أن يكون هو الإنسان ، وإما أن يكون صفة من صفاته وحالة من أحواله ، وإما أن يكون فاعلاً مستقلاً بنفسه ، قد تقدم على الأفعال باختيار نفسه . والأول باطل . لأنا بينا أن الإنسان لا يريد هذه الحالة. والثاني باطل . لأن الصفة القائمة بذات الإنسان ، لا يمكنها أن تقدم على فعل ، على خلاف إرادة الإنسان . ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت أن الفاعل لتلك الكلمات فاعلا آخر غير الإنسان ، وغير مجموع صفاته . فنقول : ذلك الفاعل لا يمكنه أن يأتي بتلك الكلمات ، إلا إذا كان عالماً قادراً نه إن لم يكن عالماً بأحوال تلك الكلمات ، يمتنع منه القصد إلى تكوينها . لأن القصد إلى ما يكون معلوماً محال . فذلك الفاعل عالم بحقائق الأشياء ، وقادر على تكوين تلك الكلمات. وهو شيء مغاير لذات الإنسان والمجموع صفاته. فذلك الشيء إما أن يكون عرضاً حالاً في جسم ، وإما أن يكون جوهراً قائماً بذاته. والأول باطل . لأن العلم الضروري حاصل بـان العرض القائم بالغير، لا يكون حياً عالماً قادراً فاعلاً للأفعال الشاقة . ولما بطل هذا ، ثبت أنه ذات قائمة بالنفس ، عالمة، قادرة على الإتيان بأفعال على خلاف إرادة الإنسان ، وقادرة على إيصال تلك الكلمات إلى باطن قلب الإنسان . ولا نريد بالجن والشياطين إلا هذا. فنحن سميناه بهذا الاسم ، وأنتم سميتموه بالقوة المفكرة . فالنزاع ليس إلا في اللفظ . وأما المعنى فمتفق عليه فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب
The philosophers said: 'Why is it not permissible that the doer of those words and those utterances is the thinking power (al-quwwah al-mufakkirah), which moves from attribute to attribute and from state to state?' And we say: This speech has been long in hearing by man, and for this reason it has become entrenched in their hearts. And it is absolutely false, and two aspects indicate it: The first: We have shown in previous articles that that which is described with all perceptions and which disposes with all kinds of dispositions in this body must be one thing, and it is the human being. And if it is like that, it is impossible to say that thought (al-fikr) does something other than the human being. For we have proven that the human being did not do these utterances because he does not intend them; rather, he dislikes them. So this speech is false. The second: This thing that you have called the thinking power: either it is the human being, or it is an attribute of his attributes and a state of his states, or it is an independent agent, having preceded the actions by his own choice. The first is false, for we have shown that the human being does not intend this state. The second is false, because an attribute subsisting in the essence of the human being cannot initiate an action contrary to the human being's will. And since these two divisions are false, it is established that the doer of those words is another doer, other than the human being and other than the totality of his attributes. So we say: That doer cannot bring about those words unless he is knowing and powerful. For if he is not knowing of the states of those words, intending to produce them is impossible because intending what is unknown is impossible. So that doer is knowing of the realities of things and powerful to produce those words. And it is something different from the essence of the human being and the totality of his attributes. Then that thing is either an accident inhering in a body or a self-subsistent substance. The first is false because necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) is obtained that an accident subsisting in another cannot be living, knowing, powerful, and doer of difficult actions. And since this is false, it is established that it is a self-subsistent essence, knowing, powerful to bring about actions contrary to the human being's will, and powerful to convey those words to the interior of the human being's heart. And we do not mean by 'jinn and devils' (al-jinn wa al-shayatin) except this. So we have named it with this name, and you have named it with the thinking power. So the disagreement is only in wording, but as for the meaning, it is agreed upon. This is the completion of the speech in this chapter.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' objection that the thinking power (al-quwwah al-mufakkirah) could be the agent responsible for producing speech and words. The author first states the objection: why can't the thinking power be the doer? He then declares it absolutely false and provides two proofs. The first proof argues that the human being is the unified subject of all perceptions and actions in the body, so thought cannot be a separate agent. The second proof uses a tripartite division: the thinking power is either the human, an attribute, or an independent agent. The first two are eliminated because the human does not will such states, and an attribute cannot act against the human's will. This leaves an independent agent, which must be knowing and powerful. It cannot be an accident (since accidents cannot perform difficult actions), so it must be a self-subsistent essence. The author identifies this essence as jinn or devils, concluding that the dispute is merely verbal—the philosophers call it 'thinking power,' while the author calls it 'jinn.' The passage thus defends the reality of spiritual agents and harmonizes the differing terminology.

Key Terms
القوة المفكرة
الفاعل
العلم الضروري
الجن والشياطين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The thinking power is a separate faculty of the human soul.
Correction: It is actually an independent spiritual entity (jinn) that can act contrary to the human will.

Checkpoint

A) It is a separate faculty of the human soul.
B) It is an attribute of God.
C) It is actually the jinn or devils.
D) It is a purely metaphorical concept.

Key Takeaway
The thinking power is not a human mental faculty but rather an independent spiritual being, identified with jinn, and the disagreement with philosophers is merely terminological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Third Proof: Empirical Experience of Spirits
Sanusi presents a third empirical proof using children and spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses empirical observation of spirit contact through children as a proof for the existence of spiritual agents.

Core Idea
Consistent empirical verification confirms the reality of spirits.

Sanusi introduces a third proof based on repeated experiments. He describes how practitioners use children to communicate with spiritual images, obtaining hidden knowledge that proves true. This empirical approach grounds the existence of jinn in observable phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: أصحاب التجارب (people of experience), الصور (images), الصبيان (children).
2
Identify the logical structure
The proof follows a sequence: experimenters use children, children see images, they ask about hidden matters, receive answers, and the answers are verified. Identify each part.
The author uses the word 'ثم' (then) to indicate sequence.
3
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider why the author considers this empirical. The repeated verification (تجربة) turns a one-time event into a reliable proof. How does this support the existence of spirits?
Some may object that it's merely imagination, but consistency is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن أصحاب التجارب قد جربوا عمل الترابي والصبيان يشاهدون حضور صور مخصوصة . والرجل يأمر الصبي ، حتى يسأل تلك الصور عن أمور مخفية ، وأحوال مكنونة . ثم إن ذلك الصبي يقول : إنهم قالوا : كذا وكذا . فإذا جرب ، وجد الأمر على وفق تلك الإنذارات . وذلك يدل على ما قلناه [ والله أعلم ] .
The third proof: Indeed, people of experience have experimented with the work of al-turabi (the earthy?) and the children witness the presence of specific images. And the man commands the child until he asks those images about hidden matters and concealed states. Then that child says: 'They said such and such.' So when he experiments, he finds the matter in accordance with those warnings. And that indicates what we have said. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof based on empirical observation. It describes how practitioners (perhaps sorcerers or spiritualists) use children to contact specific images (likely spirits or jinn). The child, under instruction, asks these images about hidden things and then reports what they say. When the experiment is repeated, the predictions come true. This empirical evidence supports the author's earlier claim about the existence of spiritual agents that can convey knowledge. The proof relies on the reliability of repeated experience (tajriba) and confirms the reality of jinn and their ability to inform humans about hidden matters.

The Reality of Jinn and the Terminological Dispute
The author defines jinn and clarifies the terminological dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author’s definition of jinn as self-subsistent essences and the nature of the disagreement with those who call them 'the thinking power'.

Core Idea
The debate over jinn is merely terminological; the reality is agreed upon.

The author concludes his discussion on jinn by clarifying both their nature and the nature of the disagreement. He affirms that jinn are real, knowing, powerful agents capable of influencing humans, and that the only dispute with opponents is the name, not the substance. This sets the stage for the evidentiary arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage describing the nature of jinn and the terminological dispute. Then read the English translation to understand the argument.
2
Identify the attributes of jinn
Note the three attributes the author gives: self-subsistence, knowledge, and power to act contrary to human will and to convey words into the heart.
These are essential characteristics.
3
Understand the dispute
The author says the disagreement is only about the word. We call it jinn, others call it the thinking power, but the meaning is agreed upon. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ، ثبت أنه ذات قائمة بالنفس، عالمة، قادرة على الإتيان بأفعال على خلاف إرادة الإنسان ، وقادرة على إيصال تلك الكلمات إلى باطن قلب الإنسان . ولا نريد بالجن والشياطين إلا هذا. فنحن سميناه بهذا الاسم ، وأنتم سميتموه بالقوة المفكرة . فالنزاع ليس إلا في اللفظ . وأما المعنى فمتفق عليه فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب .

The author presents the third proof from experience.

Waswasa and Ilham Only Affect the First Stage
Proof that whispering/inspiration affect only the first stage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why satanic whispering and angelic inspiration can only affect the initial stage of human action—the stage of knowledge or awareness—and not the later stages of inclination and power.

Core Idea
Satan can whisper, but he cannot force your will.

In this passage, the author narrows down where external influences like devilish whispers and angelic inspirations can truly have an effect. He systematically eliminates the two later stages of action—where inclination and power combine—leaving only the initial stage of knowledge and awareness. This shows that Satan cannot directly compel you to sin; at most, he can remind you of forgotten desires.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three stages mentioned: last, intermediate, first.
2
Identify the three stages
The passage examines three possible loci for whispering and inspiration: the last stage (power+inclination), intermediate stage (inclination alone), and first stage (knowledge). Write them down.
3
Understand why the last stage is impossible
The author argues that the effect of power combined with inclination on the act is necessary and inherent. The Necessary Being (God) cannot have any effect from another in it. So whispering cannot affect this stage.
This uses a principle: necessary things cannot be altered by external factors.
4
Understand why the intermediate stage is impossible
The author says that belief in benefit or harm necessarily produces will or aversion. This effect is self-essential and necessary. Therefore, external influences like waswasa cannot affect it.
The key word is 'ḍarūrī' (necessary).
5
See the conclusion and examples
Since the first two stages are impossible, the only possible effect is on the first stage. The author gives examples: Satan reminds a person of forgotten pleasure in sin, and an angel reminds of forgotten joy in obedience.
Thus, waswasa and ilham work by reminding or causing forgetfulness, not by changing inclination or power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : وسوسة الشياطين وإلهام الملك . إما أن يقعا في المرتبة الأخيرة ، أو في المرتبة المتوسطة ، أو في المرتبة الأولى . لا جائز وقوعها في المرتبة الأخيرة ، لأن تأثير مجموع القدرة مع الميل في الفعل : أمر ضروري لازم . والواجب لذاته ، يمتنع أن يكون لغيره فيه أثر . ولا جائز أيضاً أن يحصل للإلهام والوسوسة أثر في المرتبة المتوسطة . لأن تأثير اعتقاد أنه مشتمل على النفع الخالص أو الراجح في وجود إرادة الفعل : تأثير ذاتي ضروري [ وكذلك تأثير اعتقاد أنه مشتمل على الضرر الخالص أو الراجح في وجود كراهية الفعل تأثير ضروري ] والأشياء الضرورية ، يمتنع أن يكون لغيرها فيها أثر . ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، تعين أن يكون تأثير الوسوسة والإلهام في المرتبة الأولى فقط مثل : أنه كان ناسياً [ لما في المعصية الفلانية من اللذة والطيب والراحة ، فالشيطان يذكره بهذه الحالة. أو كان ناسياً لما ] في الطاعة الفلانية من البهجة والسعادة والآثار التامة الكاملة ، فالملك يذكره ذلك .
And when this is established, we say: The whispering of the devils (waswasa al-shayāṭīn) and the inspiration of the angel (ilhām al-malak) — either they occur in the last stage, or in the intermediate stage, or in the first stage. It is not possible for them to occur in the last stage, because the effect of the combination of power with inclination on the act is a necessary (ḍarūrī) and inseparable matter. And the Necessary Being (al-wājib li-dhātihi) cannot have any effect from another in it. It is also not possible for inspiration and whispering to have an effect in the intermediate stage, because the effect of the belief that something contains pure or preponderant benefit in bringing about the will to act is a self-essential, necessary effect; and likewise the effect of the belief that something contains pure or preponderant harm in bringing about aversion to the act is a necessary effect. And necessary things cannot have any effect from another in them. Since these two cases are invalid, it is determined that the effect of whispering and inspiration is only in the first stage, such as: he was forgetful of the pleasure, delight, and ease in that particular sin, so the devil reminds him of this state; or he was forgetful of the joy, happiness, and perfect, complete effects in that particular act of obedience, so the angel reminds him of that.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a proof that satanic whispering and angelic inspiration affect only the first stage of human action. The author examines the three possible loci: the final stage (power+inclination), the intermediate stage (inclination), and the initial stage (knowledge). He argues that the first two are impossible because they involve necessary effects that cannot be influenced by external factors. Therefore, the only possible effect is on the first stage, by reminding or causing forgetfulness. The author provides examples of both cases.

Key Terms

إلهام
المرتبة الأولى
ضروري
تأثير ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Satan can directly force a person to sin by overpowering their will.
Correction: Satan can only affect the first stage of knowledge by reminding or making forgetful; he cannot affect inclination or power.

Checkpoint

D) Because Satan has no power over believers

Key Takeaway
Satan and angels can only affect your knowledge by reminding or causing forgetfulness, not your will or power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Qur'anic Confirmation and the First Branch on Satanic Waswasa
Author confirms conclusion with Qur'an and presents first branch on satanic waswasa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Qur'anic verse confirms the author's earlier proof and why the first branch states that Satan cannot cast whispering without the assistance of human temperamental and psychological powers.

Core Idea
Satan's influence depends on your own inner dispositions.

In this passage, the author ties his rational proof to divine revelation by citing the Qur'anic verse in which Satan disclaims direct authority. Then he opens the first branch of his discussion, which explains that the devil's whispers are not arbitrary—they work through your own temperamental and psychological tendencies. Understanding this clarifies the nature of spiritual struggle and personal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal translation. Notice the shift from quoting the Qur'an to introducing the first branch.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the key idea is already clear.
2
Identify the Qur'anic confirmation
The author says his research confirms the truth of what is in the Divine Book. Locate the verse and understand what it adds: Satan had no authority except his call, and people responded.
This verse is from Surah Ibrahim, verse 22.
3
Examine the first branch statement
The first sub-section (al-farʿ al-awwal) states that the devil cannot cast whispering except with assistance from temperamental and psychological powers. Do you see what 'temperamental' and 'psychological' refer to?
Temperamental relates to bodily constitution; psychological relates to mental traits.
4
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that people differ in their inclinations and aversions. The devil exploits these differences. Notice the categories listed: wealth, status, foods, drinks, marriageable women, entertainments.
These are the areas where individual differences are most apparent.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider: if Satan needs your own tendencies to work, what does that mean for personal responsibility and spiritual self-discipline?
This leads to the idea that guarding one's inner state is key to resisting waswasa.

ولما تلخص هذا البحث الشريف العالي، ظهر بهذا صدق ما ورد في الكتاب الإلهي . حيث أخبر عن الشيطان أنه قال : « وما كان لي عليكم من سلطان ، إلا أن دعوتكم ، فاستجبتم لي ، فلا تلوموني ولوموا أنفسكم) وهذا بحث شريف في غاية الجلالة. ولما شرفنا الله بفضله ورحمته بالإرشاد إلى هذه الأسرار الروحانية . فلنفرع عليها ما يليق بها من التفاريع : الفرع الأول : إن الشيطان لا يقوى على إلقاء الوسوسة ، إلا بإعانة من القوى المزاجية ، والقوى النفسانية. وذلك لأنا قد بينا : أن الناس مختلفون في الميل إلى بعض الأشياء ، والنفرة عن بعض الأشياء . فالذي يريده هذا ، يبغضه ذاك ، وبالعكس. وأقسام هذه الاختلافات بحسب الرغبة في المال والجاه ، وبحسب أقسام المطعومات والمشروبات والمنكوحات . والتفرجات : مختلفة غير مضبوطة. فإذا أراد الشيطان حمله على فعل من الأفعال القبيحة ،

That is because we have shown that people differ in their inclination towards some things and aversion from some things. What one wants, another hates, and vice versa. The categories of these differences according to desire for wealth, status, and according to categories of foods, drinks, and marriageable women, and entertainments — they are varied and not fixed. So when the devil wants to incite him to commit a reprehensible act...

Commentary
This passage concludes the previous proof by showing its conformity with the Qur'anic verse where Satan disclaims authority. Then the author introduces the first branch of the discussion: the devil requires assistance from the person's own temperamental and psychological tendencies. He explains that people have different inclinations and aversions, and the devil exploits these. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: satanic influence is not direct coercion but works through pre-existing human dispositions.

Hadith of the Sun's Prostration as Evidence for Celestial Life
The author uses a hadith to argue for celestial life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of the sun's prostration is used to argue that celestial bodies are living beings with knowledge, power, and obedience.

Core Idea
Prostration requires a living, knowing subject.

The author engages directly with a common objection: that the idea of celestial life is far-fetched. He points out that those who reject it actually narrate a hadith that, if taken literally, proves the very point they deny. This passage walks through the logical inference from a well-known report to a profound theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the author's argument. Notice the contrast between the 'people of the outward' and the inference drawn from the hadith.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'because' and 'that necessitates'.
2
Identify the hadith and its key elements
The author quotes a hadith about the sun's prostration and movement. Identify the three actions: sunset journey, sunrise prostration, and rising. Why are these relevant?
3
Understand the inference from prostration to life
The author argues that prostration requires cognition (knowledge of the one being prostrated to). From knowledge, life and power follow. Trace this logical chain.
This is the core of the argument: an action that requires knowledge cannot be performed by a non-living object.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
The author concludes that the sun is an obedient 'animal' (living being). Consider how this hadith-based proof supports the overall thesis of celestial life. Then preview that Quranic verses will also be cited.
This lesson focuses on the hadith; later lessons may cover Quranic evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن أهل الظاهر إذا سمعوا هذا الكلام استبعدوه . وهذا الاستبعاد مستبعد منهم جداً . وذلك لأنهم يروون خبراً عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، أنه قال : إن الشمس عند الغروب ، يذهب بها ، إلى ما تحت العرش ، وعند الطلوع تسجد الله تعالى سجدة ، ثم تطلع ، ومعلوم أن السجود لا يصح منها إلا إذا كانت عارفة بربها. وذلك يقتضي إثبات الحياة والقدرة والعلم ، فوجب بمقتضى هذا الخبر : كون الشمس حيواناً مطيعاً الله عز وجل . وسنذكر في فصل مفرد : أن الآيات الكثيرة من القرآن تدل على أن الأمر كذلك .
Know that the people of the outward (ahl al-ẓāhir) when they hear this speech reject it. And this rejection is greatly rejected from them. That is because they narrate a report from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Indeed the sun at sunset is taken to what is beneath the Throne, and at sunrise it prostrates to Allah Almighty a prostration, then it rises. And it is known that prostration is not valid from it unless it is cognizant of its Lord.' That necessitates affirming life, power, and knowledge. So by virtue of this report, it is necessary that the sun is an obedient animal (living being) to Allah Almighty. And we will mention in a separate chapter that many verses from the Qur'an indicate that the matter is as such.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a proof from hadith. The author begins by noting that the 'people of the outward' (ahl al-ẓāhir) reject the idea that celestial bodies are living. He finds this rejection surprising because they themselves narrate a hadith that implies the sun is a living being. The hadith describes the sun's prostration to Allah and its journey under the Throne. Prostration requires knowledge of the one being prostrated to, so the sun must have knowledge. That necessitates life, power, and knowledge. Therefore, the sun is an obedient animal (i.e., living being). The author then promises to bring Quranic verses in support later.

Eighth Argument: Heavens as Dwellings of Angels
An argument that heavens are living because they are dwellings of angels, who are spirits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth argument that the heavens are living beings because they are the dwelling places of angels, who are spirits.

Core Idea
The heavens are living because angels inhabit them as spirits.

The author presents the eighth and final argument for the heavens being living. It rests on a consensus that the heavens are the homes of angels. Since angels are not physical bodies, they must be spirits that manage the heavens, analogous to how souls manage bodies. This analogy implies that the heavens possess life and rationality through their angelic inhabitants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note how consensus, the nature of angels, and analogies build the conclusion.
The passage is concise; each part is essential.
2
Identify the consensus and angelic nature
The argument starts with the agreement of rational people that heavens are angelic dwellings. Then it asserts angels are not dense bodies (to be proven later), so they must be spirits.
This logical step is crucial for the analogy that follows.
3
Understand the analogies
The author gives two analogies: heavens:angels :: bodies:spirits, and planets:angels :: hearts. Reflect on why these analogies support life and command.
Hearts are the seat of life and command in the body.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Consider how these analogies imply that the heavens are living beings with angels as their spirits. The argument concludes the chapter's discussion.

الحجة الثامنة : أطبق جمهور العقلاء : على أن السموات منازل الملائكة ومساكنهم . ولا شك أن الملائكة ليسوا أجساماً كثيفة ـ على ما ستأتي براهين ذلك - فوجب كونها أرواحاً مدبرة لتلك الأجساد ، فتكون الأفلاك بالنسبة إليها ، كالأجساد بالنسبة إلى الأرواح ، وتكون الكواكب بالنسبة إليها ، كالقلوب .. وهذا تمام ما أردنا ذكره في هذا الباب

Thus the heavens in relation to them are like bodies in relation to spirits, and the planets in relation to them are like hearts. And this is the end of what we intended to mention in this chapter.

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and final argument for the heavens being living. It begins with a consensus of rational people that the heavens are the dwelling places of angels. The heavens are therefore analogous to bodies with angels as their spirits, and the planets are analogous to hearts—the organs of life and command. This implies that the heavens possess life and rationality through the angels that inhabit them, serving as their souls. The passage concludes the chapter's arguments.

Quranic Proofs for the Life of Celestial Bodies
The author quotes two Quranic verses and argues they prove celestial beings are rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural arguments from the Quran that celestial bodies possess life and rationality.

Core Idea
The Quran's plural forms reveal that celestial bodies are rational beings.

In this passage, the author turns to the Quran to support the claim that celestial bodies are alive and rational. He presents two specific verses where the language used for the sun, moon, and celestial bodies employs plural forms reserved for intelligent beings. Let's examine these verses and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct proofs.
2
Understand the linguistic argument

This is a grammatical point.
3

The verse about the sun and moon prostrating uses the pronoun 'hum' and the plural participle 'sājidīn', again implying rationality.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might see these as metaphorical. The author insists on a literal reading as evidence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : إن الكتاب الإلهي ناطق يكون الأجرام الفلكية موصوفة بالحياة . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : قوله تعالى : ( وكل في فلك يسبحون (۳) ) والجمع بالواو والنون لا يليق إلا بالعقلاء . الثاني : قوله تعالى : ( والشمس والقمر ، رأيتهم لي ساجدين (1) )
The second discussion: The divine book speaks that the celestial bodies are described with life. And that is indicated by aspects: The first: His saying, exalted: 'And each in an orbit they swim' (Ya-Sin 36:40). The plural with waw and nun is only fitting for rational beings. Commentary
This passage is the second subsidiary discussion, providing scriptural evidence for the life of celestial bodies. The author gives two proofs from the Qur'an. First, verse 36:40: 'And each in an orbit they swim' (wa kullun fī falakin yasbaḥūn). The use of the masculine plural suffix -ūn indicates rational beings, implying that the heavenly bodies possess life and intellect. Second, verse 12:4 where Prophet Joseph says: 'I saw the sun and the moon prostrating to me' (ra'aytuhum lī sājidīn). The pronoun 'them' (hum) and the plural participle 'prostrating' (sājidīn) indicate that the sun and moon are rational beings capable of prostration, thus alive.

Three Qur'anic Proofs That Celestial Bodies Are Alive
The author enumerates three Qur'anic verses as proofs for the life and rationality of celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Qur'anic verses that the author uses to demonstrate that celestial bodies possess life and rationality.

Core Idea
The Quran's own language shows the heavens are alive and rational.

In this passage, the author presents three direct Qur'anic proofs that celestial bodies are living beings with intellect. Each proof hinges on a specific linguistic feature of the Arabic text. By examining these verses, we see that the Quran consistently describes the heavens in terms that apply only to rational, living creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice the three proofs are explicitly enumerated.
The author uses 'الأول' (first), 'الثاني' (second), 'الثالث' (third).
2
Examine the first proof from Surah Yaseen
Focus on verse 36:40: 'وكل في فلك يسبحون'. The verb 'يسبحون' uses the masculine plural suffix, which in Arabic is reserved for rational beings. This implies the celestial bodies are rational.
3
Examine the second proof from Surah Yusuf
Verse 12:4 says 'والشمس والقمر رأيتهم لي ساجدين'. The pronoun 'رأيتهم' refers to the sun and moon using a rational plural pronoun, indicating they have intellect.
4
Examine the third proof from Surah Fussilat
Verse 41:12 states 'وأوحى في كل سماء أمرها'. The act of giving a command (أمر) is only directed to living beings capable of receiving it. Thus the heavens must be alive.
5
Understand the collective conclusion
All three linguistic arguments point to life and rationality. The author concludes the discussion with 'فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب'.

Source Text

The second: His saying, the Exalted, 'And the sun and the moon, I saw them prostrating to me' (12:4), and these pronouns are only appropriate for the rational. The third: His saying, the Exalted, 'And He inspired in each heaven its command' (41:12), and command is only directed to the living. So this is the end of the speech in this chapter.

Commentary

Third, from verse 41:12 ("وأوحى في كل سماء أمرها"), the act of giving a command (أمر) is only directed to living beings capable of receiving and executing it. Thus, all three verses imply that the celestial bodies are alive and rational. The author concludes the section with 'فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب' (this is the end of the speech in this chapter).

Razi's First Argument: The Celestial Soul Cannot Be Both Physical and Spiritual
Razi presents the first argument against the claim that celestial souls must be physical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first reductio argument showing that the opponent's claim that celestial souls must be physical leads to an impossibility.

Core Idea
Razi traps the opponent: one thing cannot be both physical and abstract.

Razi begins his critique with a powerful reductio ad absurdum. He takes the opponent's own premise—that the celestial soul's purpose is assimilation to the intellect—and shows that it forces the soul to be both a physical force and an abstract substance. The passage presents the argument and then outlines the only possible escapes, each of which undermines the opponent's system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three premises
The argument relies on three key premises: the soul's purpose, the requirement for perception of abstracts, and the nature of partial perceptions. Find each in the text.
3
Understand the reductio
See how combining these premises forces the soul to be both physical and abstract, which is impossible. This is the core of the argument.
The impossibility is a contradiction in terms.
4
Explore the escape routes
Razi lists three possible ways the opponent could deny a premise. Consider how each would undermine their own established principles.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Why does Razi consider this argument decisive? Because it uses the opponent's own accepted premises to reach an absurd conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن غرض النفس عندكم من تحريك الفلك هو التشبه بالعقل ، على ما سيأتي تفسير هذا التشبه . وإرادة التشبه بالغير ، مشروطة بمعرفة صفة ذلك الغير، فوجب أن تكون النفس المحركة لجسم السماء ، عالمة بالعقل المجرد . فثبت : أن الشيء الذي يباشر تحريك . الفلك ، هو الذي يكون مدركاً لذلك العقل المجرد . [ ولكمالاته . وإدراك المجردات لا يصح إلا من الجوهر المجرد (۱) ] فيلزم : أن تكون النفس الفلكية لكونها صاحبة الإدراكات الجزئية : قوة جسمانية ، ولكونها مدركة للعقل المجرد : جوهراً مجرداً ، فالشيء الواحد جسماني ومجرد . وذلك باطل قطعاً . واعلم أنه لا خلاص عن هذا الكلام، إلا بالقدح في إحدى مقدمات ثلاث : إما أن يقولوا : صاحب الإدراكات الجزئية ، لا يجب أن يكون جسمانياً ، أو يقولوا : ليس الغرض للنفس من تحريك الفلك هو التشبه بالعقل ، أو يقولوا : القوة الجسمانية يصح عليها إدراك المجردات . [ وأي واحد (۲) ] من هذه الثلاثة ، قالوا به : فقد تركوا أصلا من أصولهم المشهورة ، وقاعدة من قواعدهم المعتبرة .
First: The purpose of the soul according to you in moving the sphere is assimilation to the intellect, as the interpretation of this assimilation will come later. And the will to assimilate to another is conditional upon knowing the attribute of that other, so it is necessary that the soul that moves the body of the heaven be knowledgeable of the abstract intellect. Thus it is established that the thing that directly moves the sphere is the one that perceives that abstract intellect. [And its perfections. And perception of abstract things is not valid except from an abstract substance (1)] Therefore, it follows that the celestial soul, because it possesses partial perceptions, is a physical force, and because it perceives the abstract intellect, is an abstract substance. So one thing is physical and abstract. And that is absolutely invalid. And know that there is no escape from this statement except by impugning one of three premises: either they say that the possessor of partial perceptions does not have to be physical, or they say that the purpose of the soul in moving the sphere is not assimilation to the intellect, or they say that the physical force can perceive abstract things. And whichever one of these three they adopt, they have abandoned a well-known principle of theirs and a respected foundation of their rules.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the claim that the possessor of partial perceptions must be a physical force. The author begins by stating a premise accepted by his opponents: the soul's purpose in moving the sphere is to resemble the intellect. This requires knowledge of the intellect, so the moving soul must perceive the abstract intellect. Since perception of abstract things can only be by an abstract substance, the celestial soul must be abstract. But it also has partial perceptions, which according to the opponent must be physical. Thus one thing would be both physical and abstract, which is impossible. The author then notes that the only escape is to deny one of three premises: either partial perceptions don't require physicality, or the soul's purpose is not resemblance to the intellect, or a physical force can perceive abstracts. Any choice undermines the opponent's own principles. The argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
نفس فلكية
إدراكات جزئية
إدراكات كلية
عقل مجرد
جوهر مجرد
قوة جسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only works if the opponent accepts the premises, making it a strawman.
Correction: Razi uses premises that his opponents actually hold, so the argument is a valid refutation from their own principles.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, what conclusion follows if the celestial soul has partial perceptions and also perceives the abstract intellect?
A) The soul is purely spiritual.
B) The soul is a physical force only.
C) The soul would have to be both physical and abstract, which is impossible.
D) The soul cannot perceive the intellect.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument forces a contradiction from the opponent's own premises, showing the impossibility of the celestial soul being both physical and spiritual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when followed to its logical conclusion, forced you to accept something contradictory? What did you do—ignore it, or face it? Razi forces the opponent to face their inconsistency. Will you make a habit of testing your own faith-claims for internal consistency, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The opponent's premise—that the soul's purpose is assimilation to the intellect—is accepted without proof. Are there premises in your own understanding of God, the soul, or the afterlife that you have simply assumed without questioning? Will you search the Quran and Sunnah today for evidence to confirm or correct them?
?
Razi's argument shows that a false premise leads to an absurd conclusion. Consider your own beliefs about the nature of the soul. If you hold that the soul is physical, what contradictions might emerge? If you hold it is spiritual, does your life reflect that belief? Examine your priorities today: do you live as if your soul is merely physical, or as if it is a spiritual reality destined for eternity?

The Celestial Soul: Abstract and Perceptive
Razi states his preferred view on the celestial soul's nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's view that the celestial soul is an abstract substance with both partial and universal perceptions and wills, and how this resolves theological doubts.

Core Idea
The celestial soul is non-corporeal yet knows partials and universals.

In this passage, Razi declares his own chosen view on the nature of the celestial soul. He affirms that it is a purely spiritual substance, not physical, and that it possesses both particular and universal knowledge and will. This allows it to worship its Lord consciously. This formulation, he claims, clears away the doubts raised by earlier arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's declaration
Start by reading the Arabic passage where Razi states 'المختار عندي' – his chosen view. Notice that he defines the celestial soul as an abstract substance.
This is the author's personal position, not a universally accepted one.
2
Identify the two types of perception
Find where Razi says the soul has both partial and universal perceptions and wills. Understand that this dual capacity is central to his view.
Partial = particular, concrete; Universal = general, abstract.
3
Connect to worship
Notice how Razi links these perceptions to the soul's purpose: it knows its Lord and aims its movements at worship. This shows the teleological dimension.
The movements of celestial spheres are acts of worship.
4
Reflect on the resolution of doubts
Razi concludes that on this basis, all doubts and ambiguities disappear. Consider why this view might solve earlier problems about celestial motion and divine governance.
The doubts likely refer to whether celestial bodies are animate or inanimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمختار عندي : أن النفس الفلكية جوهر مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها . هذا ، فإنها موصوفة بالإدراكات الجزئية، والإرادات الجزئية . وكذلك أيضاً موصوفة بالإدراكات الكلية، والإرادات الكلية . فهي عارفة بربها ، وقاصدة بهذه الحركات : عبادة ربها وخالقها ومدبرها. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فقد زالت الشكوك والشبهات. ويتفرع على ما ذكرنا : فروع . وهي : أن المسمى بنفس الكل هي نفس الفلك الأعظم . وذلك لأن سائر الأفلاك موجودة في باطن الفلك الأعظم . فهي كالأجزاء من الفلك الأعظم . ونفوسهـا كالقوى المتشعبة من نفس الفلك الأعظم ، كما أن القوى المتشعبة عن النفس الناطقة الموجودة في كل واحد من الأعضاء المخصوصة كالنتائج والآثار والشعب لجوهر النفس الناطقة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن تدبير الحق سبحانه لعالم الأجسام ، إنما ابتدأ من الفلك الأعظم، الذي هو العرش. وذلك الابتداء إنما حصل بواسطة تلك النفس التي هي النفس الفلكية . وأما سائر النفوس الفلكية والعنصرية ، نتائجها وشعبها وأولادها. وإليه الإشارة في الدعاء المشهور وهو قوله عليه السلام : اللهم إني أسألك بمعاقد العز من عرشك ، فالمراد من معاقد العز : هو جوهر تلك النفس الكلية وشعبها ، المنبثة منها في أجزاء العرش .
And the chosen view in my opinion is that the celestial soul is an abstract substance, devoid of corporeality and its attachments. Indeed, it is described with partial perceptions and partial wills, and likewise described with universal perceptions and universal wills. So it is knowledgeable of its Lord, and aims by these movements at worship of its Lord, Creator, and Governor. On this basis, doubts and ambiguities disappear. And from what we have mentioned branch off branches: namely, that what is called the 'soul of the whole' is the soul of the greatest sphere. Because the other spheres exist within the interior of the greatest sphere, so they are like parts of the greatest sphere. And their souls are like forces branching from the soul of the greatest sphere, just as the forces branching from the rational soul present in each of the particular limbs are like results, effects, and branches of the substance of the rational soul. Thus it is established from what we have mentioned that the management of the world of bodies by the Truth, Exalted, began from the greatest sphere, which is the Throne. And that beginning occurred by means of that soul which is the celestial soul. As for other celestial and elemental souls, they are its results, branches, and children. And to this points the well-known supplication, his saying, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, I ask You by the strongholds of might from Your Throne.' So by 'strongholds of might' is meant the substance of that universal soul and its branches spread out in the parts of the Throne.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
In this passage, the author presents his own preferred view. He affirms that the celestial soul (al-nafs al-falakiyya) is an abstract substance, not physical, and it possesses both partial and universal perceptions and wills. This allows it to know its Lord and to perform its movements as worship. This resolves the doubts raised by the opponents. Then he introduces a further branch: the concept of the 'soul of the whole' (nafs al-kull), which is the soul of the greatest sphere (the Throne). All other spheres are contained within it like parts, and their souls are like branches of that one soul, analogous to the rational soul's branching into various faculties. This implies that the divine management of the corporeal world begins from the Throne via this soul. He then cites a famous supplication of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that refers to 'strongholds of might on Your Throne,' interpreting that as the substance and branches of the universal soul within the Throne. This is a theological cosmogony rooted in philosophical Sufism and kalam.

Key Terms
نفس فلكية
جوهر مجرد
إدراكات جزئية
إدراكات كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think celestial souls are purely universal and cannot perceive particulars.
Correction: Razi explicitly states they have both partial and universal perceptions and wills.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's preferred view, what types of perception does the celestial soul have?
A) Only partial perceptions
B) Only universal perceptions
C) Both partial and universal perceptions
D) Neither partial nor universal perceptions

Key Takeaway
Razi affirms that the celestial soul is an abstract substance possessing both particular and universal knowledge, enabling it to worship its Lord and resolve theological doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The celestial soul engages in its motions as an act of worship. Examine your daily routines: which of your actions are truly acts of worship aimed at your Creator, and which are merely habitual? What would it take to transform one recurring movement in your day into a conscious act of worship?
?
Razi asserts that the celestial soul knows its Lord intimately. How much time and effort do you devote to knowing your Lord compared to your engagement with other knowledge? Is your pursuit of knowledge aligned with the purpose of knowing Him?
?
The celestial soul resolves doubts by its nature and purpose. What persistent doubt or ambiguity in your faith might be resolved by recognizing that your soul, like the celestial soul, is created for worship? How would embracing that purpose change how you approach that doubt?

From Nafs al-Kull to the Throne: Cosmic Hierarchy
Razi explains the hierarchy of celestial souls starting from the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi derives the hierarchy of celestial souls, with the 'soul of the whole' as the soul of the greatest sphere (the Throne), and how other souls are its branches.

Core Idea
All celestial souls are branches of the one soul of the greatest sphere.

Having established the nature of the celestial soul, Razi now develops a cosmological hierarchy. He identifies the 'soul of the whole' as the soul of the greatest sphere, which is the Throne. All other spheres and their souls are contained within it like parts and branch-forces. This leads to a unified divine governance starting from the Throne, and Razi supports it with a prophetic supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the branching passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'ويتفرع على ما ذكرنا'. Notice that Razi is now deriving further conclusions from his earlier view.
This is a theological amplification, not a new proof.
2
Identify the soul of the whole
Find where Razi defines al-musammā bi-nafs al-kull as the soul of the greatest sphere. Understand that 'the whole' refers to the entire cosmos.
Nafs al-kull is a term from Neoplatonic philosophy.
3
Understand the containment analogy
Razi says other spheres are inside the greatest sphere like parts, and their souls are like forces branching from it. Compare this to the rational soul's powers in the body.
This implies a hierarchical emanation.
4
Connect to the Throne and supplication
Razi states that divine management began from the greatest sphere (the Throne) via this soul. Then he interprets the supplication 'bi-ma‘āqid al-‘iz min ‘arshika' as referring to the universal soul and its branches.
This ties his philosophical view to Islamic scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the chosen view in my opinion is that the celestial soul is an abstract substance, devoid of corporeality and its attachments. Indeed, it is described with partial perceptions and partial wills, and likewise described with universal perceptions and universal wills. So it is knowledgeable of its Lord, and aims by these movements at worship of its Lord, Creator, and Governor. On this basis, doubts and ambiguities disappear. And from what we have mentioned branch off branches: namely, that what is called the 'soul of the whole' is the soul of the greatest sphere. Because the other spheres exist within the interior of the greatest sphere, so they are like parts of the greatest sphere. And their souls are like forces branching from the soul of the greatest sphere, just as the forces branching from the rational soul present in each of the particular limbs are like results, effects, and branches of the substance of the rational soul. Thus it is established from what we have mentioned that the management of the world of bodies by the Truth, Exalted, began from the greatest sphere, which is the Throne. And that beginning occurred by means of that soul which is the celestial soul. As for other celestial and elemental souls, they are its results, branches, and children. And to this points the well-known supplication, his saying, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, I ask You by the strongholds of might from Your Throne.' So by 'strongholds of might' is meant the substance of that universal soul and its branches spread out in the parts of the Throne.

Commentary

He affirms that the celestial soul (al-nafs al-falakiyya) is an abstract substance, not physical, and it possesses both partial and universal perceptions and wills. Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the relationship between the souls of other spheres and the soul of the greatest sphere?
A) They are completely separate and independent.
B) They are like forces branching from the soul of the greatest sphere.
C) They are identical to the soul of the greatest sphere.
D) They are physical parts of the greatest sphere.

Key Takeaway
Razi establishes a hierarchical cosmology where the soul of the greatest sphere (Throne) is the source of all other celestial souls, which are its branches, and this is supported by the Prophet's supplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Prophet's supplication 'O Allah, I ask You by the strongholds of might from Your Throne,' do you ever pause to consider the cosmic hierarchy Razi unveils — that your own soul is a distant branch of that universal soul? How does this awareness reshape the weight of your words when you pray?
?
If all celestial souls are but branches of the soul of the Throne, and your own soul is a result of that same emanation, then what does that imply about the interconnectedness of all existence? In what specific ways can you live today as if you truly believe that your actions affect the whole cosmos?
?
Razi argues that divine management began from the Throne via its soul. Do you trust that same management in the minutiae of your life — in your struggles, your decisions, your breath? If not, which area of your life are you still trying to manage independently, and will you consciously surrender it to the One who governs from the Throne?

The Truth About the Number of Angels
The author concludes with Quranic verses affirming limited human knowledge of the angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran declares the number of angels to be known only to Allah, refuting any human attempt to fix it.

Core Idea
Only Allah knows the full number of His angels.

This brief but powerful passage concludes the discussion on angels. After various philosophical arguments, the author turns to the Quran as the ultimate authority, citing two verses that establish a clear limit: human knowledge is insufficient, and only God knows the 'soldiers' of the Lord.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice its brevity and conclusive tone.
This is the final word on the matter.
2
Understand the context
This passage ends a debate about the number of angels (intellects and souls). The author is stating the correct view according to revelation.
3
Analyze the verses
The first verse (17:85) says humans have been given little knowledge. The second (74:31) says none knows the soldiers of your Lord except Him. Reflect on how together they establish the point.
4
Apply the lesson
Recognize that in Islamic theology, certain matters are beyond human inquiry and must be left to divine revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق في هذا الباب : ما جاء في الكتاب الإلمي حيث قال : « وما أوتيتم من العلم إلا قليلا »، وقال : ( وما يعلم جنود ربك إلا هو ) .
And the truth in this matter is what came in the Divine Book where He said: 'And you have not been given of knowledge except a little' (Quran 17:85), and He said: 'And none knows the soldiers of your Lord except Him' (Quran 74:31).
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage states the final position: the correct view regarding the number of angels (intellects and souls) is what the Quran declares. The author cites two verses: first, 'you have not been given of knowledge except a little'—human knowledge is limited; second, 'none knows the soldiers of your Lord except Him'—only God knows the full number of His angels. This serves as a definitive refutation of any human attempt to fix the number of celestial intellects and souls.

Key Terms
ملائكة الله
جنود ربك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is relying solely on rational arguments.
Correction: Here he grounds the conclusion in revelation as the ultimate authority, citing Quranic verses.

Checkpoint
What is the author's final authority for the number of angels?
A) The astronomers' observations.
B) The philosophers' emanation scheme.
C) The Quranic verses that limit human knowledge.
D) The consensus of the theologians.

Key Takeaway
The definitive truth about the number of angels is that only Allah knows it; human knowledge is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter matters that only Allah knows—like the exact number of angels—does your heart submit to His exclusive knowledge, or do you feel a desire to speculate beyond what He has revealed? What does that reveal about the state of your tawheed?
?
How often do you find yourself trying to know the 'full picture' in your life—whether about the future, other people, or unseen realities—when Allah has only given you a little knowledge? How can this verse reset your intellectual humility and reliance on revelation?
?
In your daily life, are there areas where you claim knowledge or certainty that Allah has not given you? What would it look like to consciously submit those areas to Allah, saying, 'You alone know the full truth'?

Three Views on the Nature of Angels
Razi enumerates three views on the nature of angels after stating consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical views on the nature of angels as categorized by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, and how they are classified by whether angels are quantified or not.

Core Idea
Angels are either quantified bodies or not; three views arise from this distinction.

Razi opens his discussion of angels by noting a universal agreement on their nobility, then immediately turns to the disputed question of their quiddity. He organizes the debate through a simple binary: are angels quantified (occupying space) or not? Under quantification, he lists three major views. This passage sets the stage for the chapter's detailed analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing for analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's structure come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the binary classification
Notice how Razi first states that people differ on the quiddity of angels, then presents a binary: they are either mutahayyiza (quantified) or not. This is the key to organizing the discussion.
Only the first division (quantified) is elaborated with three views in this passage.
3
Examine the first view
The view of most Muslims: angels are delicate, airy, luminous bodies with specific attributes (able to change forms, eternal, etc.). How does this compare to common modern conceptions?
This view is usually associated with Ash'ari and Maturidi schools.
4
Contrast the second and third views
The second view identifies angels with planets (good and bad fortune), the third with dualistic principles (light and darkness). Note how these are radically different from the first view.
Razi does not endorse any view here; he simply presents them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن في خرج صفات الملائكة بحسب ما وجدنا في الكتاب الكي[؟] اعلم : أنه لا خلاف بين العقلاء في أن أشرف أقسام الحسن والكمال للعالم العلوي هو وجود الملائكة فيه. كما أن أشرف أقسام الزينة في العالم الأسفل ، هو وجود الإنسان العاقل فيه . إلا أن الناس اختلفوا في ماهية الملائكة . وطريق ضبط الأقوال أن نقول : إنها إما أن تكون متحيزة ، أو لا تكون متحيزة أما القسم الأول . ففيه أقوال : الأول : إنها أجسام لطيفة هوائية نورانية ، تقدر على التشكل بأشكال مختلفة وأنها ذوات باقية ، مبرأة عن الذبول والانحلال والضعف والاختلال ومسكنها السموات . وهذا قول أكثر المسلمين . الثاني : قول طوائف من عبدة الأصنام. وهو أن الملائكة في الحقيقة هي هذه الكواكب ، الموصوفة بالإسعاد والإنحاس. فإنها أحياء ناطقة . فالسعود ملائكة الرحمة ، والنحوس ملائكة العذاب . الثالث : قول الثنوية . وهو أن هذا العالم مركب من أصلين قديمين .
The Eighth Chapter: On the Emergence of the Attributes of Angels according to what we have found in the Noble Book. Know: there is no disagreement among rational people that the noblest division of beauty and perfection in the upper world is the existence of angels in it, just as the noblest division of adornment in the lower world is the existence of the rational human being in it. However, people have differed concerning the quiddity (mahiyya) of angels. The way to control the sayings is to say: they are either quantified (mutahayyiza) or not quantified. As for the first division, there are sayings concerning it: First: they are delicate, airy, luminous bodies, capable of assuming different forms, and they are enduring essences, free from withering, dissolution, weakness, and disorder, and their abode is the heavens. This is the saying of most Muslims. Second: the saying of groups of idol worshippers—that the angels in reality are these planets, described as bringing good fortune and bad fortune, for they are living, speaking beings. The fortunate ones are angels of mercy, and the unfortunate ones are angels of punishment. Third: the saying of the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya)—that this world is composed of two eternal principles.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage opens the eighth chapter on the attributes of angels. The author first establishes a consensus: the noblest element in the upper world is the existence of angels, just as the noblest in the lower world is rational humans. Then he notes disagreement on the quiddity (mahiyya) of angels. He proposes a classification: angels are either quantified (mutahayyiza, i.e., occupying space) or not. Under the first division (quantified), he lists three views. The first view (of most Muslims) is that angels are delicate, airy, luminous bodies capable of assuming forms, eternal, and residing in the heavens. The second view (of idol worshippers) identifies angels with the planets, which are living, rational beings—auspicious planets are mercy angels, inauspicious ones are punishment angels. The third view (of the Dualists) holds that the world is composed of two eternal principles (light and darkness), likely implying angels are emanations from these principles. The passage thus outlines the framework for the chapter's discussion.

Key Terms
ماهية الملائكة
متحيزة
أجسام لطيفة هوائية نورانية
الكواكب
الإسعاد والإنحاس
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi endorses the Muslim view as correct.
Correction: Razi presents all three views neutrally for analysis; he does not indicate preference here. The phrase 'saying of most Muslims' is descriptive, not prescriptive.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Muslims, what is the nature of angels?
A) They are the planets themselves
B) They are delicate, airy, luminous bodies
C) They are dualistic principles
D) They are purely spiritual without bodies

Key Takeaway
Razi classifies views on angels by whether they are quantified bodies, listing Muslim, idolatrous, and dualist perspectives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that the noblest adornment in the lower world is the existence of rational humans. Do you truly live as if your rationality is a trust from Allah, or do you squander it on what is lower? What specific habit will you change today to honor this gift?
?
The idol worshippers believed angels were planets that determine fortune. In what subtle ways do you attribute power to created things—your job, your health, your social status—rather than seeing them as instruments under Allah's command? Will you repent of any hidden shirk in your heart?
?
The Dualists saw the world as composed of two eternal principles. Do you ever act as if good and evil come from independent sources, forgetting that both are from Allah? How will you remind yourself daily that Allah alone is the Creator of all things?

The Three Materialist Views of Angels
Describes three materialist views of angels as bodies of light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three materialist views that consider angels as bodies composed of light and darkness, and the attributes associated with these substances.

Core Idea
Materialists reduce angels and devils to substances of light and darkness.

This passage presents the materialist position on angels, which is a dualistic cosmology of light and darkness. The author explains that these are three statements all agreeing that angels are material bodies. We'll examine the description of the two substances and their generated beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall sense of the materialist view.
2
Identify the two substances
The passage mentions two substances: light (النور) and darkness (الظلمة). Note their attributes.
3
Analyze the attributes of light
Light is described as virtuous, good, pure, sweet-scented, noble. It generates the saints (angels) by a process of emanation.
The generation is not sexual but like wisdom from the wise.
4
Understand the three statements
The author says 'these three statements' — implying there are three variations among materialists who all hold that angels are material bodies. The passage only describes one variation in detail.
The other two statements are not elaborated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(2) وهما النور والظلمة . وهما جوهران حساسان مختاران قادران ، متضادان في النفس والصورة ، مختلفان قادران ، في الفعل والتدبير . فجوهر النور فاضل خير نقي طيب الريح كريم النفس ، يسر ولا يضر ، وينفع ولا يمنع ، ويحيى ولا يبلى . وأما جوهر الظلمة فهو على ضد ذلك . ثم إن جوهر النور لم يزل يولد الأولياء . وهم الملائكة ، لا على سبيل التناكح ، بل على سبيل تولد الحكمة من الحكيم ، والضوء من المضيء . وجوهر الظلمة لم يزل يولد الأعداء [ وهم الشياطين ] لا على سبيل التناكح ، بل على سبيل تولد السفه من السفيه . فهذه الأقوال الثلاثة هي أقوال من جعل الملائكة أشياء متحيزة جسمانية .
(2) And they are light and darkness. They are two substances, sentient, volitional, powerful, opposite in essence and form, different in action and management. The substance of light is virtuous, good, pure, sweet-scented, noble in essence, it makes easy and does not harm, benefits and does not withhold, gives life and does not perish. As for the substance of darkness, it is the opposite of that. Then the substance of light continuously generates the saints, who are the angels, not by way of sexual reproduction, but by way of the generation of wisdom from the wise, and light from the luminous. And the substance of darkness continuously generates the enemies, [who are the devils], not by way of sexual reproduction, but by way of the generation of folly from the foolish. So these three statements are the statements of those who consider the angels to be material bodily things.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage begins by describing the views of those who take angels to be material bodies. The author first presents a dualistic view positing two eternal substances: light and darkness. Each substance has attributes: light is good, darkness evil. Angels are generated from light, devils from darkness. Then the author states that these are three statements (implying there are three variations among materialists) all agreeing that angels are material and bodily. The author does not elaborate the other two statements here.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author only presents one materialist view, but he refers to three distinct statements.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'these three statements are the statements of those who consider angels to be material bodily things', meaning there are three variations among materialists.

Checkpoint
What are the two substances from which angels and devils are said to be generated according to the materialist view presented?
A) Fire and water
B) Light and darkness
C) Spirit and matter
D) Good and evil

Key Takeaway
Materialists viewed angels and devils as material bodies generated from the substances of light and darkness, respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself reducing spiritual realities (like angels or Satan) to mere physical or psychological explanations? This is exactly what the materialist view does. Confess honestly: when was the last time you dismissed the unseen realm because it didn't fit your material assumptions? What step will you take this week to actively affirm the reality of spiritual beings?
?
The materialist sees angels as products of a substance, not as chosen servants of Allah. Do you sometimes treat your own faith as a product of your environment or upbringing, rather than a conscious response to the Creator? If you truly believe angels are real beings, how should that change the way you live today?
?
If you were to explain to a skeptic that angels are not made of light as a physical substance, what exact wording from this passage would you use to distinguish between the materialist view and Islamic belief? Write down your answer and test it on a friend this week.
